Why Hearing Is Not Many Powers Like Touch
Taftazani responds to the analogy between touch and hearing regarding multiplicity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key distinction between hearing and touch that prevents the analogy from making hearing multiple powers.

Core Idea
Hearing's primary object is sound; touch senses each opposite directly.

The objector argues: if touch is multiple because it perceives multiple opposites, then hearing should also be multiple because it perceives opposites in sound. Taftazani's answer reveals a crucial difference: hearing's primary object is sound itself, and the opposites are mere accidents of sound, whereas touch senses each opposite directly. This short but powerful distinction preserves the unity of hearing.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic and translation of the objection. Notice the analogy: touch is many powers because it perceives many opposites; hearing perceives many opposites (heavy/sharp sound, faint/loud sound), so why isn't it many?
The objector is using the same reasoning applied earlier to touch.
2
Read the answer given by Taftazani
Read the answer in Arabic and translation. Pay attention to the phrase 'its primary sensible is sound'.
3
Identify the core distinction
Notice the contrast: for hearing, the opposites are accidents of sound (the primary object). For touch, each opposite is sensed directly as itself, not as an accident of some primary object.
This is the key insight that breaks the analogy.
4
Reflect on why the analogy fails
Ask yourself: if hearing's opposites were not accidents but were sensed directly, then hearing would need multiple powers. But they are accidents, so a single power suffices. Touch's opposites are direct, so it needs multiple powers.
The argument turns on the distinction between primary and accidental sensibles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال : فإن قيل : فالسمع أيضاً يدرك المضادة التي بين الصوت الثقيل والحاد، والتي بين الصوت الخافت والجهير وغير ذلك ، فلم لم تجعل قوى كثيرة .
فالجواب أن محسوسه الأول هو الصوت، وهذه أعراض لها، وتوابع بخلاف اللمس فإن كل واحدة من المتضادات تحس لذاتها، لا بسبب الاخر
Then he said: If it is said: Hearing also perceives the opposition between heavy and sharp sound, and between faint and loud sound, and other things, so why is it not made many powers? The answer is that its primary sensible is sound, and these are accidents and attributes of it, unlike touch, for each of the opposites is sensed for itself, not by means of another.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage answers an objection. The objector argues: if touch is multiple because it perceives multiple opposites (hot/cold, wet/dry, etc.), then hearing should also be multiple because it perceives multiple opposites in sound (heavy/sharp, faint/loud, etc.). The answer distinguishes: hearing's primary object is sound itself; the differing qualities are accidents of sound. In touch, each opposite (e.g., hot, cold) is sensed directly as itself, not as a property of something else. Hence the analogy fails.

Key Terms
السمع
المضادة
محسوسه الأول
الصوت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That hearing's perception of multiple sound qualities implies hearing should be multiple faculties, just as touch is multiple.
Correction: Hearing's primary object is sound; the qualities are accidents of sound. Touch senses each opposite directly, so touch requires multiple powers. Hearing does not.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say hearing is not multiple like touch?
A) Because hearing has only one organ.
B) Because its primary object is sound, and qualities are accidental.
C) Because sound does not have actual opposites.
D) Because hearing is a single power by consensus.

Key Takeaway
The analogy between hearing and touch fails because hearing's primary object is sound and its opposites are accidental, whereas touch directly senses each opposite as itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When someone speaks to you in a loud or sharp tone, do you immediately react to the tone itself, or do you pause to consider the meaning behind it? How does this lesson's distinction between the primary object and accidental qualities help you avoid reacting over the wrong thing?
?
Think of a recent disagreement where you focused on the way something was said rather than what was said. Could recognizing the sound as an accident of the message have changed your response? What will you do differently next time?
?
If hearing's primary object is sound, and qualities are accidents, then what does that tell you about the weight you give to tone, volume, or intonation in your daily conversations? Are you treating accidents as if they are the essence?

Why Taste is a Single Sense: The Argument from Opposition Type
Taftazani explains why taste is one faculty despite multiple tastes, unlike touch.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why taste is considered a single sense despite perceiving many different flavors, based on the idea of a single type of opposition.

Core Idea
Taste has one kind of opposition; touch has many. That's why taste is one sense.

This lesson addresses a subtle point about the sense of taste. How can taste be a single faculty when it perceives many opposites like sweet, sour, bitter? Taftazani relays an answer from Imam al-Razi: all tastes share a single oppositional relationship. In contrast, touch involves fundamentally different kinds of opposition, justifying its division into separate faculties.

Guided Path
1
Understand the problem
The passage begins by noting that taste perceives multiple opposing tastes. Consider why this might suggest taste should be multiple senses.
Think of how touch has separate faculties for hot/cold and wet/dry.
2
Learn al-Razi's answer
Read the answer: all tastes have a single opposition type. This is the key reason taste is one faculty.
The phrase 'مضادة واحدة' is crucial.
3
Compare with touch
Touch has multiple opposition types (hot/cold vs. wet/dry). This contrast clarifies why touch is multiple and taste is single.
Reflect on why this difference matters for defining the senses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما كان السؤال في الذوق المدرك للطعوم المتضادة ظاهراً.
أجاب الإمام بأن الطعوم وإن كثرت فبينها مضادة واحدة، بخلاف الملموسات، فإن بين الحرارة والبرودة نوعاً من التضاد، غير النوع الذي بين
الرطوبة واليبوسة .
And when the question concerning taste, which perceives opposed tastes, was apparent, the Imam answered that tastes, even if many, have between them a single opposition, unlike tangible things, for between heat and cold there is a kind of opposition different from the kind which is between wetness and dryness.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage addresses a potential objection: taste, like touch, perceives many opposites (sweet, sour, bitter, etc.), so why is taste not multiple faculties? The answer (attributed to 'the Imam', likely al-Razi) is that all tastes share a single type of opposition. For example, sweet and bitter are opposites of the same generic kind. In contrast, touch involves fundamentally different types of opposition (hot/cold, wet/dry, etc.), each a distinct genus. Thus taste remains one faculty.

Key Terms
الذوق
الطعوم
المضادة الواحدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Since taste perceives many different flavors (sweet, sour, bitter, etc.), it must be multiple senses like touch.
Correction: All tastes share a single type of opposition, so they are perceived by one sense. Touch has multiple types of opposition, justifying multiple senses.

Checkpoint
Why is taste considered a single sense despite perceiving many tastes?
A) Because all tastes reduce to one opposite pair.
B) Because it uses a single organ.
C) Because tastes are not truly opposites.
D) Because it is a universal sense.

Key Takeaway
Taste is a single sense because all its sensations share one generic type of opposition, unlike touch.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You experience many inner conflicts—desires, fears, doubts. Do you treat them as a single opposition (good vs. evil) or as unrelated struggles? How does the lesson's principle of 'single opposition type' challenge your view of your own spiritual coherence?
?
The argument shows that unity does not come from the number of experiences but from the nature of their relationship. What is the one underlying opposition that unifies your life's choices? If you cannot find it, what would it take to discover it?
?
Taste remains one despite perceiving many flavors. Your heart perceives many joys and sorrows. Are they all opposed in the same way to the presence of Allah? Or do you treat some as neutral? Be honest: what is the true opposition that defines your relationship with your Creator?

Critique of Multiple Tactile Powers
The author critiques the basis for multiple tactile powers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical debate about whether multiple tactile qualities require multiple powers and the author's criticism of weak arguments.

Core Idea
Multiple tactile qualities do not necessarily imply multiple powers.

In this passage, the author critically examines the argument that the diversity of tangible opposites requires multiple faculties of touch. He analyzes the reasoning behind the philosophers' position and points out a flawed argument based on primary versus secondary qualities. Finally, he raises a deeper question about the nature of perception and opposition.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' claim
Begin by reading the passage carefully. Notice that the philosophers assert that each type of opposition is governed by a single power called sensation and discrimination.
2
Analyze the two interpretations
The author considers two possible reasons for the diversity of tactile opposites: either due to accidental relations or due to intrinsic opposition. Understand the implications of each.
This step helps you see the logical structure of the argument.
3
Identify the weak argument
The author dismisses an argument based on the disparity between primary and secondary qualities. Identify why this argument is considered foolish.
4
Reflect on the further inquiry
The author raises a point: senses perceive opposites, not opposition itself, which is intellectual. Consider how this undermines the basis for multiple powers.
This connects to the core idea.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحكماء أوجبوا أن يكون الحاكم على كل نوع من أنواع التضاد قوة واحدة تسمى بالشعور والتمييز. وأنت خبير بأن دعوى تنوع التضاد في الملموسات إن كانت من جهة أن تنوع المعروضات يوجب تنوع الإضافات العارضة، فالكل سواء. وإن كانت بالنظر إلى نفس التضاد العارض ، فلا يتم بدون برهان ،وتفرقة، ومن سخيف الكلام ما قيل أن تباين الكيفيات الأول. أعني الحرارة والبرودة والرطوبة واليبوسة، أشد من تباين الكيفيات الثواني الحادثة من تفاعلها كالروايح والألوان والطعوم، فلذلك تعددت قوى اللمس، دون باقي الحواس . وههنا بحث آخر، وهو أن المدرك بالحس هو المتضادات كالحرارة والبرودة دون التضاد، فإنه من المعاني العقلية، فكيف جعلوا مبنى تعدد اللامسة، تعدد أنواع التضاد، وجوزوا إدراك القوة الواحدة للمدركات المتضادة، كالباصرة للسواد والبياض . ولم يجعلوا ذلك أفعالاً مختلفة من مبدأ واحد بالذات والاعتبار.
The philosophers held that the governor over each type of opposition is a single power called sensation and discrimination. You know well that if the claim of diversity of opposition in tangible things is from the perspective that the diversity of subjects necessitates diversity of accidental relations, then all are the same. And if it is with respect to the opposition itself that attaches, then it is not complete without proof and distinction. Among the foolish speech is what was said: the disparity of the primary qualities—I mean heat, cold, moisture, and dryness—is greater than the disparity of the secondary qualities arising from their interaction, such as smells, colors, and tastes, therefore the powers of touch are multiplied, unlike the other senses. And here is another inquiry: namely, that what is perceived by sense is the opposites such as heat and cold, not opposition, for it is among intellectual meanings. So how did they make the basis of multiplicity of touch the multiplicity of types of opposition, and allow the perception of opposite perceptibles by a single power, like the sight perceiving black and white, and did not consider those different acts from a single principle essentially and by consideration?
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage is an argumentative discussion on the basis for multiple tactile senses. The author begins by stating the philosophers' view that each type of opposition is governed by a single power called sensation and discrimination. Then he analyzes the claim that the diversity of tangible oppositions requires multiple powers. He argues that if the diversity is due to the diversity of accidental relations, then all senses are the same in that respect; if it is due to the opposition itself, then it requires a proof and a distinction. He then dismisses a weak argument that the primary qualities are more disparate than the secondary ones, which is why touch has multiple powers but other senses do not. Finally, he raises another point: the senses perceive opposites, not opposition itself, which is intellectual. So how can one use the diversity of types of opposition as the basis for multiple tactile powers, considering that a single power like sight can perceive opposites like black and white? The author implies that the argument for multiple powers is flawed.

Key Terms
تضاد
قوة
لمس
حكماء
شعور
تمييز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Multiple tactile qualities automatically require multiple powers.
Correction: The author argues that this claim is not proven; it either reduces to triviality or requires proof, and a common argument based on quality disparity is flawed.

Checkpoint
What is the author's main criticism of the argument that tactile qualities require multiple powers?
A) The argument lacks proof and distinction between accidental relations and intrinsic opposition.
B) The philosophers all agreed that one power suffices.
C) The primary qualities are not actually disparate.
D) The sense of touch is not governed by a single power.

Key Takeaway
The diversity of tactile opposites does not straightforwardly imply a multiplicity of powers, and the rationale for such an inference is subject to scrutiny.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage exposes a weak argument that seemed plausible at first glance. Think of a belief you hold about your deen that you accepted because it 'felt right' or 'sounded convincing'—but have you ever truly examined its foundations? Will you now look closer, even if it means unsettling what you thought was certain?
?
The author points out that what is perceived by sense are the opposites themselves, not opposition—an intellectual concept. How often do you confuse an intellectual abstraction for a direct sensory or spiritual experience? Where in your life are you treating your own mental constructs as if they were revealed truths?
?
If the philosophers' reasoning for multiple tactile powers can be dismantled so thoroughly, what does that say about your own willingness to challenge authoritative-sounding claims? Are you ready to question even the 'well-known' arguments in your own faith tradition, not to reject them, but to understand their true strength? Commit to writing down one such claim this week and examining it against the sources.

Definition and Mechanism of Taste
The author defines the sense of taste and explains its mechanism with two theories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the sense of taste according to classical Islamic thought and the two theories explaining how saliva mediates taste.

Core Idea
Taste requires a medium — but is it a carrier or a transformer?

In this passage, the author defines the sense of taste and its mechanism. He first gives the anatomical and physiological definition, then explains its benefit, and finally presents a scholarly dispute over how saliva mediates taste. This lesson will help you grasp the subtlety of sensory perception in classical kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Pay attention to key terms like ذوق, قوة, رطوبة لعابية.
2
Identify the definition of taste
Find the sentence that defines taste. Note the essential conditions: contact, mediation of saliva, and the condition that saliva must be tasteless.
3
Compare taste with touch
The author contrasts taste with touch. Why does taste require a medium while touch does not? What is the difference in how they function?
This sets up the problem that the two theories aim to solve.
4
Analyze the two theories
The passage presents two opinions on how saliva mediates taste. Distinguish between the 'mixing' theory and the 'transformation' theory. What is the role of saliva in each?
These theories reflect different views on perception and the nature of qualities.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this dispute matters. How does it relate to broader questions in theology and philosophy about how we perceive the world?
This connects to discussions about the reality of sensible qualities and the role of media.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (ومنها الذوق) : وهي قوة منبثة في العصب المفروش على جرم اللسان، تدرك بها الطعوم بشرط المماسة، وتوسط الرطوبة اللعابية، وخلوها عن المثل والضد ليتكيف بكيفية الطعوم أو تخالطها أجزاء منه فيغوص. هو تال اللمس في المنفعة، بحيث يفعل ما به يتقوم البدن، وهو تشهية الغذاء و اختايره، ويوافقه في الاحتياج إلى الملامسة ويفارقه في أن نفس الملامسة، لا تؤدي الطعم، كما أن نفس ملامسة الحار، تؤدي إلى الحرارة ، بل لا بد من توسط الرطوبة اللعابية المنبعثة من الآلة المسماة بالملعبة بشرط خلوها عن طعم، وإلا لم تؤد الطعم لصحة كما في بعض الأمراض، واختلفوا في أن توسطها. بان يخالطها أجزاء ذي الطعم مخالطة ينتشر فيها ، ثم ينفذ فيغوص في اللسان ، حتى يخالط اللسان فيحسه أو بأن يستحيل نفس الرطوبة إلى كيفية المطعوم، ويقبل الطعم منه من غير مخالطة . فعلى الأول . تكون الرطوبة واسطة تسهل وصول جوهر المحسوس الحامل للكيفية إلى الحاس، ويكون الإحساس بملامسة الحاس للمحسوس بلا واسطة . وعلى الثاني. يكون المحسوس بالحقيقة هو الرطوبة، ويكون بلا واسطة
He said: 'And among them is taste.' It is a power diffused in the nerve spread over the substance of the tongue, by which tastes are perceived on condition of contact and the mediation of salivary moisture, and its freedom from a similar or opposite so that it may be qualified by the quality of tastes or parts of it may mix with it and then penetrate. It is next to touch in benefit, such that it does what sustains the body, which is appetizing food and choosing it. It agrees with touch in needing contact and differs in that mere contact does not convey taste, just as mere contact with hot conveys heat. Rather, the mediation of salivary moisture emanating from the instrument called the salivary gland is necessary, on condition that it is free from taste; otherwise it does not convey taste correctly, as in some diseases. They disputed about its mediation: either by it mixing with parts of the thing having taste, a mixture in which it spreads, then penetrates and sinks into the tongue until it mixes with the tongue and senses it; or by the moisture itself being transformed into the quality of the tasted thing and receiving the taste from it without mixing. According to the first, the moisture is a medium facilitating the arrival of the substance of the sensed object carrying the quality to the sense, and the sensation is by contact of the sense with the sensed without a medium. According to the second, the sensed thing in reality is the moisture, and it is without a medium.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage defines the sense of taste and explains its mechanism. It first gives the definition: a power distributed in the nerve covering the tongue, perceiving tastes through contact and mediation of saliva, which must be free of any taste itself. Then it describes the benefit of taste: it appetizes and selects food. It compares taste to touch: both require contact, but touch directly senses heat, while taste requires a medium (saliva). It then presents two opinions on how the saliva mediates: (1) by mixing with the tasted substance and carrying it to the tongue; (2) by the saliva itself transforming into the taste quality. The first opinion sees the saliva as a medium that facilitates the passage of the tasted substance to the sense, while the second sees the saliva itself as the direct object of sensation.

Key Terms
ذوق
قوة
لسان
رطوبة لعابية
ملعبة
مماسة
تشهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Taste works by simple contact, like touch.
Correction: Taste requires a medium (saliva) and the medium must be tasteless. The role of saliva is debated: it either carries the tasted substance or transforms into the taste.

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the role of saliva in taste?
A) It directly conveys the taste to the tongue by mixing.
B) It is either a medium transmitting the tasted substance or it transforms into the taste itself.
C) It is unnecessary for taste.
D) It must have a taste of its own to be effective.

Key Takeaway
The sense of taste involves a medium (saliva) that either carries or transforms the taste, highlighting the complexity of sensory perception in classical thought.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You rely on your sense of taste every day to choose what you eat. How often do you pause to consider that your saliva is not just a lubricant but an active participant in allowing you to taste? When you eat your next meal, will you silently thank Allah for this intricate mechanism and ask yourself whether you are using this blessing to choose food that nourishes both body and soul?
?
The author says taste is 'next to touch in benefit' because it helps appetize and select food. Think of a time you ate something purely for pleasure, ignoring its nutritional value. Did your sense of taste lead you astray? What would need to change in your heart so that your taste serves your body's true needs rather than your cravings?
?
Both theories of taste mediation involve a transformation or mixing that makes the external quality accessible to you. This means you never taste an object directly; you taste what your own body has prepared. Does this remind you that your perception of reality is always mediated? How can this awareness humble you and push you to seek truth beyond your immediate sensations?

Taste and Touch: When Sensations Combine
The author explains how taste and touch qualities can combine and be indistinguishable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how taste and touch can combine in the tongue, producing compound sensations that are perceived as single tastes.

Core Idea
Some tastes are actually blends of flavor and feel.

In this passage, the author explores the fascinating interplay between taste and touch. While we often think of flavor as purely gustatory, the tongue also senses texture and temperature. The author explains how these can combine, sometimes seamlessly, to create a unified perception.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how the author uses examples to illustrate the concept.
2
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two cases: either the taste and touch effects are distinguishable in sensation, or they are not. Find these in the text.
3
Examine the examples
The author gives three examples of compound sensations: pungency, sourness, and astringency. For each, note which tactile quality (cutting, heating, drying) combines with taste.
Think of familiar foods that exhibit these qualities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (وما في اللسان : قد يتميز أثرهما، كالحلاوة والحرارة، وقد لا يتميز به. يعني أن المطعومات كما تفيد ذوقاً، فقد يفيد بعضها لمساً، أما مع تميزه في الحس، كما في الحلو الحار، وإما بدونه. وحينئذ يتركب من الكيفية الطعمية ، ومن التأثير اللمس شيء واحد، يصير كطعم محض مثل الحرافة، فإنها طعم مع تفريق واسخان ، وكالحموضة ، فإنها طعم مع تفريق بلا اسخان، والكعفوصة فإنها طعم مع تجفيف أو تكثيف.
He said: 'And what is in the tongue: their effect may be distinguished, like sweetness and heat, and may not be distinguished.' He means that foodstuffs as they convey taste, some of them also convey touch, either with distinction in sensation, as in hot sweet, or without it. And then a single thing is composed from the taste quality and the tactile effect, becoming like a pure taste, such as pungency, for it is taste with a cutting and heating effect; and sourness, for it is taste with a cutting effect without heating; and astringency, for it is taste with drying or condensing.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage discusses the interaction between taste and touch when perceiving food. The author begins by quoting a statement: 'their effect may be distinguished, like sweetness and heat, and may not be distinguished.' Then he explains: some foods provide both a taste and a tactile sensation. Sometimes these are distinguishable (e.g., something sweet and hot), sometimes not. In the latter case, a single compound sensation results, appearing as a pure taste. Examples: pungency (حرافة) is taste with cutting and heating; sourness (حموضة) is taste with cutting without heating; astringency (عفوصة) is taste with drying or condensing. This shows how the sense of taste and touch can cooperate and produce unified perceptions.

Key Terms
ذوق
لمس
حرافة
حموضة
عفوصة
كيفية طعمية
تأثير لمسي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Taste is only about flavor, not touch.
Correction: Some tastes like pungency include tactile components such as heat or cutting, showing that touch and taste can blend.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of a compound sensation that combines taste and touch?
A) Pure sweetness
B) Pungency (حرافة)
C) Coldness
D) Smoothness

Key Takeaway
Taste and touch are not always separate; they can merge into unified sensations like pungency, sourness, and astringency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a sensation like sourness or astringency, do you ever stop to consider that your tongue is giving you both a taste and a physical touch? How might this awareness change the way you interpret your own body's signals? This week, every time you eat something sour or pungent, pause intentionally to identify the tactile component. What does that practice teach you about paying attention to the subtle layers in your spiritual life?
?
The author shows that some sensations we think are simple are actually composites. Can you recall a time when you mistook a complex emotion or feeling for a simple one? How can the lesson's framework help you discern what is really going on inside you, especially in matters of faith and sincerity? What step will you take today to look beyond the surface of your feelings?

Two Theories on How Moisture Mediates Taste Sensation
Two theories on moisture mediation in taste.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two competing theories about the role of moisture in taste perception and the implications for direct vs. mediated sensation.

Core Idea
Moisture either carries taste particles or transforms into taste itself.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid examines a subtle debate in sensory physiology: how does the moisture on the tongue enable us to taste? Two theories are proposed, each with different implications for whether sensation is direct or mediated. Let's explore them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without overanalyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's presentation of the two theories sink in.
Notice the use of 'either...or' structure.
2
Identify the two theories
The passage presents two views. The first involves mixing of moisture with tasted particles; the second involves transformation of moisture itself. Underline key phrases.
The first theory uses 'yukhalit' (mixes), the second 'yastahil' (transforms).
3
Compare their mechanisms
Ask: In the first theory, what is the role of moisture? In the second, what becomes the direct object of sensation? Consider how each theory explains direct vs. indirect contact.
4
Consider the consequences
Reflect on why this debate matters for kalam: it touches on how we perceive qualities and whether a medium is necessary. The first theory has a medium; the second does not.
This connects to broader discussions of causality and perception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإلا لم تؤد الطعم لصحة كما في بعض الأمراض، واختلفوا في أن توسطها. بان يخالطها أجزاء ذي الطعم مخالطة ينتشر فيها ، ثم ينفذ فيغوص في اللسان ، حتى يخالط اللسان فيحسه أو بأن يستحيل نفس الرطوبة إلى كيفية المطعوم، ويقبل الطعم منه من غير مخالطة . فعلى الأول . تكون الرطوبة واسطة تسهل وصول جوهر المحسوس الحامل للكيفية إلى الحاس، ويكون الإحساس بملامسة الحاس للمحسوس بلا واسطة . وعلى الثاني. يكون المحسوس بالحقيقة هو الرطوبة، ويكون بلا واسطة
Else it would not convey taste correctly, as in some diseases. They differed regarding its mediation. Either it mixes with particles of the tasted substance in a mixture that spreads, then penetrates and dips into the tongue until it mixes with the tongue and senses it, or the moisture itself transforms into the quality of the tasted thing and receives taste from it without mixing. On the first, the moisture acts as a medium facilitating the arrival of the essence of the sensed object carrying the quality to the sense organ, and sensation occurs by contact of the sense organ with the sensed object without a medium. On the second, the sensed object in reality is the moisture, and it is without a medium.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage presents a debate about the role of moisture on the tongue in taste perception. The author describes two theories: (1) The moisture mixes with particles of the tasted substance and carries them to the tongue, acting as a medium. (2) The moisture itself transforms into the taste quality without mixing. The first theory posits that the moisture facilitates the contact between the sense organ and the sensed object, but the actual sensation is direct contact. The second theory holds that the moisture itself becomes the object of sensation, so there is no indirect mediator. The passage illustrates a typical kalam argument about the mechanics of sensory perception.

Combined Sensations of Taste and Touch in Food Qualities
The author illustrates the combined action of taste and touch on the tongue with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how taste and touch can combine in food qualities, and how some sensations like pungency involve both senses indistinguishably.

Core Idea
Taste and touch can blend into a single sensation like pungency.

In this passage, the author explores a fascinating nuance: the tongue has both taste and touch senses, and sometimes they work together so seamlessly that we perceive a single quality. We will see examples of when these senses are distinct and when they are blended.

Guided Path

2
Identify the two possibilities

3
Examine the three examples of indistinct blending
Look at pungency, sourness, and astringency. Each combines taste with a tactile component (fragmentation, heating, drying, etc.). Understand how they form a single perceived quality.

This passage discusses how the senses of taste and touch on the tongue can produce combined qualities. Sometimes the effects of each sense are distinguishable, as in something both sweet and hot. The author gives examples: pungency (ḥurāfah) includes taste plus fragmentation and heating; sourness (ḥumūḍah) includes taste plus fragmentation without heating; astringency (kaʽfūṣah) includes taste plus drying or thickening. The Sense of Smell: Definition and Three Theories
Definition of smell and three theories of odor perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of smell as a faculty and the three theories of odor perception along with their refutations.

Core Idea
Smell is perceived through conditioned air, not particles or direct action.

The author begins by defining the sense of smell as a faculty in the forebrain. He then presents three competing theories about how odors reach our nose. The majority view holds that air conditioned with the odor quality travels to the olfactory organ, while two alternative views are refuted with empirical arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then compare with the literal translation. Let the author's reasoning unfold.
2
Identify the definition of smell
Notice that the author defines smell as a faculty located in two protrusions of the forebrain. This is the anatomical basis.
The definition includes the phrase 'قوة في زائدتي مقدم الدماغ'.
3
List the three theories
The passage presents three ways odors are perceived: (1) by air carrying the quality, (2) by evaporation and particles, and (3) by direct action without air change.
The author states the majority view first, then two alternative views.
4
Understand the refutations
The author refutes the second and third theories using the example of musk: its weight does not decrease over time (against particle emission) and its scent travels far (against direct action).
The passage ends with a counter-argument from particle theorists, but the refutations are clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (ومنها الشم) وهي قوة في زائدتي مقدم الدماغ تدرك بها الروائح، بأن يصل إليهما الهواء المتكيف بها، لا أجزاء تنفصل عن ذي الرائحة، وإلا انتقص وزنه، وحجمه بكثرة شمه. نعم، قد يعين انفصال الأجزاء البخارية على تكيف الهواء بسرعة، وكثرة اللمس على تحلل رطوبات المشمومات ولذا تهاج الروائح بالحر، وتذبل التفاحة بالشم، ولا بأن يؤثر المشمومات في الشامة من غير استحالة في الهواء، وإلا لما أدرك الرائحة من حضر، بعد زوال المشموم، وأما أنه كيف يفعل ذو الرائحة في فراسخ، والنار مع شدة تأثيرها لا تسخن إلا ما يقرب منها فمجرد استبعاد]. الجمهور على أن إدراك الروائح بوصول الهواء المتكيف بكيفية ذي الرائحة إلى آلة الشم. وقيل بتبخر وانفصال أجزاء من ذي الرائحة، تخالط الأجزاء الهوائية، فتصل إلى الشامة . وقيل : بفعل ذي الرائحة في الشامة من غير استحالة في الهواء ولا تبخر وانفصال أجزاء . ورد الثاني بأن القليل من المسك يشم على طول الأزمنة، وكثرة الأمكنة، من غير نقصان في وزنه ،وحجمه، فلو كان الشم بالتبخر، وانفصال الأجزاء لما أمكن ذلك. والثالث بأن المسك قد يذهب به إلى مسافة بعيدة جداً، أن رائحته تدرك في الهواء الأول أزمنة متطاولة. أو يحرق ويفني بالكلية مع تمسك الفريق الثاني بأن الشم لو لم يكن بالتبخر، وتحلل الأجزاء اللطيفة وانفصالها من ذي الرائحة لما كانت الحرارة وما يهيجها من الدلك والتبخر
He said: And among them is smell. It is a faculty in the two processes of the forebrain by which odors are perceived, in that the air that has taken on the odor quality reaches them, not particles separating from the odoriferous object; otherwise its weight and volume would decrease with much smelling. Yes, the separation of vaporous particles may assist in quickly conditioning the air, and much handling may help dissolve the moistures of the smelled things, hence smells are agitated by heat, and the apple wilts by smelling. And not that the smelled objects affect the olfactory organ without a change in the air; otherwise, someone present would not perceive the smell after the removal of the smelled object. As for how the odoriferous object can act from leagues away, while fire with its strong effect does not heat except what is near it, that is mere improbability. The majority hold that the perception of odors is by the arrival of air conditioned with the quality of the odoriferous object to the olfactory organ. And it is said by evaporation and separation of particles from the odoriferous object, mixing with air particles, then reaching the olfactory organ. And it is said by the action of the odoriferous object on the olfactory organ without a change in the air or evaporation and separation of particles. The second view is refuted because a little musk can be smelled over long periods and many places without decrease in its weight and volume; if smell were by evaporation and separation of particles, that would not be possible. The third view is refuted because musk may be carried to a very far distance, so that its odor is perceived in the first air for prolonged periods. Or it may burn and be completely annihilated, with the second party insisting that if smell were not by evaporation and dissolution of fine particles and their separation from the odoriferous object, then heat and what agitates it, like friction and evaporation, would not occur.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage defines the sense of smell and critiques three theories. The author first defines smell as a faculty located in two protrusions at the front of the brain. Odors are perceived when air carrying the odor quality reaches them. The author rejects particle emission: otherwise the object's weight would decrease. However, vaporous particles can help condition the air quickly. The third theory (direct action without air change) is rejected because smell would vanish instantly after the object is removed. The author then presents the majority view (air carries the odor quality). Two alternative views are described: (1) particles separate from the object and mix with air; (2) the object acts directly without air change. The second view is refuted by musk's persistence without weight loss. The third is refuted by musk's ability to be smelled at great distances. The passage ends with a counter-argument from particle theorists: if evaporation were not involved, heat and friction would not release odors.

الرائحة
الهواء المتكيف
التبخر

الشامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That smell always involves particles leaving the object.
Correction: The majority view holds that smell is due to air carrying the odor quality, not particle emission, as shown by musk's weight remaining constant.

Checkpoint

B) By direct action of the object on the nose
C) By air carrying the odor quality reaching the olfactory organ
D) By the object itself moving toward the nose

Key Takeaway
The majority view that smell is due to conditioned air reaching the olfactory organ is supported by empirical evidence against particle emission and direct action.

Definition of Smell and Three Theories
Al-Taftazani defines smell and enumerates three theories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Taftazani's definition of smell and the three competing theories of odor perception presented in the Maqasid.

Core Idea
Smell is perceived through conditioned air, not by particles or direct action.

In this passage, al-Taftazani defines the sense of smell and clarifies its proper mechanism. He first establishes that odor perception occurs via air conditioned by the odorous substance, then refutes two alternative explanations, and finally enumerates three theories. This lesson will help you grasp the classical Islamic understanding of olfactory perception and the reasoning behind the majority view.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic definition
Start by reading the Arabic passage carefully. Notice how al-Taftazani defines smell as a power in the brain and the mechanism of air reaching the olfactory organ.
Pay attention to the phrase 'بأن يصل إليهما الهواء المتكيف بها'.
2
Understand the refutations
Al-Taftazani rejects two incorrect ideas: that smell involves material particles separating from the object, and that the object acts directly on the olfactory nerve without air. Understand why each is rejected.
The particle theory would cause weight loss over time; the direct action theory would fail to explain lingering smell after removal.
3
Identify the three theories
List the three theories mentioned: (1) majority view: conditioned air arrives; (2) evaporation and particle separation; (3) direct action without air or evaporation. Notice which one is endorsed.
The majority view is introduced with 'الجمهور على أن...'.
4
Reflect on the reasoning
Consider why al-Taftazani favors the majority view. The passage shows that the other views have empirical or logical weaknesses.
Think about the apple withering example as evidence against pure particle separation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (ومنها الشم) وهي قوة في زائدتي مقدم الدماغ تدرك بها الروائح، بأن يصل إليهما الهواء المتكيف بها، لا أجزاء تنفصل عن ذي الرائحة، وإلا انتقص وزنه وحجمه بكثرة شمه. نعم، قد يعين انفصال الأجزاء البخارية على تكيف الهواء بسرعة، وكثرة اللمس على تحلل رطوبات المشمومات ولذا تهاج الروائح بالحر، وتذبل التفاحة بالشم، ولا بأن يؤثر المشمومات في الشامة من غير استحالة في الهواء، وإلا لما أدرك الرائحة من حضر، بعد زوال المشموم، وأما أنه كيف يفعل ذو الرائحة في فراسخ، والنار مع شدة تأثيرها لا تسخن إلا ما يقرب منها فمجرد استبعاد. الجمهور على أن إدراك الروائح بوصول الهواء المتكيف بكيفية ذي الرائحة إلى آلة الشم. وقيل بتبخر وانفصال أجزاء من ذي الرائحة، تخالط الأجزاء الهوائية، فتصل إلى الشامة. وقيل: بفعل ذي الرائحة في الشامة من غير استحالة في الهواء ولا تبخر وانفصال أجزاء.
He said: (And among them is smell). It is a power in the two anterior parts of the brain by which odors are perceived, such that the air conditioned by them reaches them, not particles separating from the odoriferous thing, otherwise its weight and volume would decrease by much smelling. Yes, the separation of vaporous particles may aid the conditioning of the air quickly, and much touching may aid the dissolution of the moistures of smelled things, hence odors are aroused by heat, and the apple withers by smelling. And not that the smelled things affect the olfactory nerve without change in the air, otherwise the one present would not perceive the odor after the removal of the smelled thing. As for how the odoriferous thing acts over leagues, while fire with its intense effect does not heat except what is near it, that is mere improbability. The majority hold that the perception of odors is by the arrival of the air conditioned with the quality of the odoriferous thing to the olfactory organ. And it is said: by evaporation and separation of parts from the odoriferous thing that mix with the airy parts and reach the olfactory nerve. And it is said: by the action of the odoriferous thing on the olfactory nerve without change in the air and without evaporation and separation of parts.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage defines the sense of smell and presents three competing theories regarding how odor perception occurs. The author begins by quoting someone (likely the author of the original Maqasid) defining smell as a power located in the two anterior parts of the brain. The definition states that odors are perceived when air that has been conditioned by the odorous substance reaches these parts. The author then clarifies that it is not by particles separating from the odorous substance, because that would cause a decrease in weight and volume with repeated smelling. However, he concedes that the separation of vaporous particles may aid in the rapid conditioning of the air, and that frequent contact helps dissolve the moistures of smelled objects, which is why heat intensifies odors and an apple withers with repeated sniffing. The passage then rejects the idea that the odorous substance acts directly on the olfactory nerve without any change in the air, because then a person would not perceive an odor after the source is removed. The author dismisses the objection of how an odor can travel far while fire only heats nearby as mere improbability. He then lists the three theories: first, the majority view that odor is perceived by air conditioned by the quality of the odorous substance reaching the olfactory organ; second, the evaporation theory that particles of the odorous substance evaporate, mix with air, and reach the olfactory nerve; third, the direct action theory that the odorous substance acts on the olfactory nerve without change in the air or separation of parts.

الهواء المتكيف
تبخر
انفصال الأجزاء

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many people think that odor perception involves tiny particles of the smelly object breaking off and entering the nose, or that the object directly affects the nose without any air medium.
Correction: The author argues that if particles separated, the object would lose weight over time, and if direct action occurred, the smell would disappear as soon as the object is removed. Instead, the majority view is that air conditioned by the object reaches the olfactory organ.

Refutation of Evaporation Theory
Taftazani refutes evaporation theory with musk's stable weight.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the evaporation theory of smell is falsified by the observation that musk does not lose weight or volume when smelled repeatedly.

Core Idea
Musk's constant weight disproves the evaporation theory of smell.

Al-Taftazani presents a straightforward empirical argument against the idea that smell occurs by the separation of particles from the odorous substance. Using musk as an example, he shows that if evaporation were the mechanism, the substance would lose mass over time, which is not observed. This lesson walks through the argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. The passage is a single sentence that presents the argument against the evaporation theory.
Notice that the author first states the empirical fact, then draws the logical conclusion.
2
Identify the empirical observation
The author observes that a small amount of musk can be smelled over long times and in many places without any decrease in weight or volume. This is the key data.
3
Understand the logical inference
If smell were caused by evaporation and separation of parts, then the substance would gradually lose weight. Since musk does not, the theory is falsified.
This is a modus tollens argument: if theory implies weight loss, but no weight loss, then theory false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورد الثاني بأن القليل من المسك يشم على طول الأزمنة، وكثرة الأمكنة، من غير نقصان في وزنه وحجمه، فلو كان الشم بالتبخر وانفصال الأجزاء لما أمكن ذلك. والثالث بأن المسك قد يذهب به إلى مسافة بعيدة جداً، أن رائحته تدرك في الهواء الأول أزمنة متطاولة.
And he refuted the second [theory] by [saying]: a little musk is smelled over long times and many places without any decrease in its weight and volume, so if smelling were by evaporation and separation of parts, that would not be possible. And the third [theory] by [saying]: musk may be taken to a very far distance, that its odor is perceived in the same air for prolonged times.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage presents the refutation of the second and third theories of smell. For the second theory (evaporation and separation of parts), the author argues that if smell occurred by the evaporation of particles from the odorous substance, then a substance like musk would gradually lose weight and volume over time when repeatedly smelled. However, observation shows that a small amount of musk can be smelled over long periods and in many places without any measurable decrease in weight or volume. Therefore, the evaporation theory is falsified. For the third theory (direct action of the odorous substance on the olfactory nerve without any change in the air), the author argues that if the odor were produced solely by the direct action of the substance on the nerve, then once the substance is removed, the odor should cease immediately. But we observe that the scent of musk can be carried to very distant locations and still be perceived in the same air for extended periods. This demonstrates that the air itself must carry the odor, contradicting the direct action theory.

Key Terms
المسك
نقصان في الوزن
انفصال الأجزاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that evaporation is necessary for smell, or that musk eventually wears out.
Correction: The passage shows that substantial amounts of musk can be smelled repeatedly without measurable loss, disproving the necessity of evaporation.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the evaporation theory using the example of musk?
A) Because musk loses weight when smelled repeatedly.
B) Because musk does not lose weight or volume despite being smelled many times.
C) Because musk is too heavy to evaporate.
D) Because musk has a very strong odor.

Key Takeaway
The constancy of musk's weight shows that smell does not require evaporation of material parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Taftazani used a simple everyday observation to challenge a prevailing scientific view. What simple truth are you ignoring today because it doesn't fit your assumptions? Will you act on it?

Refutation of Direct Influence Theory
Taftazani refutes direct influence theory with musk's distant perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the direct influence theory of smell is falsified by the observation that musk's scent can be perceived far away in the same air for long periods after the musk is removed.

Core Idea
Musk's distant scent proves the air carries odor, not just direct nerve contact.

The third theory claims that smell is purely a direct action of the odorous substance on the olfactory nerve without any involvement of the air. Al-Taftazani counters this with the observation that musk can be taken away and still be smelled in the same air at a distance. This shows the air retains the odor, contradicting the direct action theory.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. This is a short but clear argument against the direct influence theory.
The passage is one sentence that presents a scenario and its implication.
2
Understand the scenario
Musk is taken to a very far distance, yet its odor is perceived in the original air for extended periods. This means the smell remains in the air even after the source is removed.
This observation is based on common experience with strong scents.
3
Identify the contradiction
If smell were solely a direct action of the substance on the nerve, then once the substance is moved away, the smell should disappear from the air completely. But it does not. Therefore, the air must play a role in carrying the odor.
This rebuts the direct influence theory and supports the conditioned air theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورد الثاني بأن القليل من المسك يشم على طول الأزمنة، وكثرة الأمكنة، من غير نقصان في وزنه وحجمه، فلو كان الشم بالتبخر وانفصال الأجزاء لما أمكن ذلك. والثالث بأن المسك قد يذهب به إلى مسافة بعيدة جداً، أن رائحته تدرك في الهواء الأول أزمنة متطاولة.
And he refuted the second [theory] by [saying]: a little musk is smelled over long times and many places without any decrease in its weight and volume, so if smelling were by evaporation and separation of parts, that would not be possible. And the third [theory] by [saying]: musk may be taken to a very far distance, that its odor is perceived in the same air for prolonged times.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage presents the refutation of the second and third theories of smell. For the second theory (evaporation and separation of parts), the author argues that if smell occurred by the evaporation of particles from the odorous substance, then a substance like musk would gradually lose weight and volume over time when repeatedly smelled. However, observation shows that a small amount of musk can be smelled over long periods and in many places without any measurable decrease in weight or volume. Therefore, the evaporation theory is falsified. For the third theory (direct action of the odorous substance on the olfactory nerve without any change in the air), the author argues that if the odor were produced solely by the direct action of the substance on the nerve, then once the substance is removed, the odor should cease immediately. But we observe that the scent of musk can be carried to very distant locations and still be perceived in the same air for extended periods. This demonstrates that the air itself must carry the odor, contradicting the direct action theory.

الهواء الأول

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Musk loses weight when smelled often.
B) Musk's odor is perceived in the same air after the musk is removed.
C) Musk cannot be smelled at a distance.
D) Musk has no smell at all.

Key Takeaway
The persistence of musk's scent in the air after removal shows that smell is carried by the air, not just direct nerve contact.

Objection from Evaporation Proponents and Answer
Objection from evaporation proponents and Al-Taftazani's response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Taftazani refutes the evaporation theory's claim that observable phenomena prove smell is caused by particle evaporation.

Core Idea
Observations do not prove evaporation as the cause of smell; they only show it assists.

The proponents of the evaporation theory argue that if smell were not caused by the separation of fine particles, we would not observe that heat intensifies odors, cold suppresses them, or an apple wilts from repeated sniffing. These observations seem to support their theory. However, Al-Taftazani responds by denying that these phenomena necessarily imply evaporation as the direct mechanism. In this lesson, we examine the objection and its careful refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the passage from the beginning through 'واللازم باطل بحكم المشاهدة'. Identify the three empirical phenomena the objectors present as evidence.
The objectors claim heat, friction, cold, and sniffing affect odors – these are observable.
2
Understand the implied logic
The objectors reason: if evaporation were not the cause, then these phenomena would not occur. Since they do occur, evaporation must be the cause. This is a conditional argument.
The 'لازم' (consequent) is the false conclusion if the premise were not true.
3
Examine the answer
Read Al-Taftazani's answer: 'والجواب منع الملازمة...' . He denies the logical connection, not the observations themselves. He offers an alternative explanation.
He says evaporation aids conditioning of air, and the apple's withering is due to moisture loss.
4
Reflect on the distinction
Consider why this refutation is important: it shows that correlation does not imply causation, especially in theological arguments about the nature of perception.
This lesson guards against assuming that physical processes are the direct cause of sensory experience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو يحرق ويفني بالكلية مع تمسك الفريق الثاني بأن الشم لو لم يكن بالتبخر وتحلل الأجزاء اللطيفة وانفصالها من ذي الرائحة لما كانت الحرارة وما يهيجها من الدلك والتبخر تذكى الروائح، ولما كان البرد الشديد يخفيها، ولما ذبلت التفاحة بكثرة التشمم. واللازم باطل بحكم المشاهدة. والجواب منع الملازمة لجواز أن يكون ذلك من جهة أن التبخر وتحلل الأجزاء يعين على تكيف الهواء بكيفية ذي الرائحة، وكثرة اللمس والتشمم على ذبول التفاحة وتحلل رطوباتها.
Or it burns and annihilates completely – along with the adherence of the second group that if smelling were not by evaporation and dissolution of fine parts and their separation from the odoriferous thing, then heat and what stimulates it of rubbing and evaporation would not intensify odors, and severe cold would not conceal them, and the apple would not wither by much sniffing. But the consequent is false by the judgment of observation. And the answer is: the implication is denied, because it is possible that that is from the aspect that evaporation and dissolution of parts aid the conditioning of the air with the quality of the odoriferous thing, and much touching and sniffing aid the withering of the apple and the dissolution of its moistures.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from the proponents of the evaporation theory (the second group) and the author's answer. The objectors argue that if smell were not caused by evaporation and the separation of fine particles from the odorous substance, then we would not observe that heat and friction (which stimulate evaporation) intensify odors, nor that intense cold suppresses odors, nor that an apple wilts as a result of repeated sniffing. Since these are observed facts, they claim that the evaporation theory must be correct. The author refutes this by denying the logical implication. He explains that it is not necessary that evaporation is the direct cause of smell; rather, evaporation and the dissolution of particles can be merely an aid in conditioning the air with the quality of the odorous substance. Similarly, the wilting of the apple is due to the dissolution of its moistures caused by repeated touching and sniffing, not directly by the mechanism of smell. Thus, the observation does not prove the evaporation theory exclusively; it only shows that evaporation assists the process, not that it is the essential cause of smell perception.

Key Terms
تبخر
تحلل الأجزاء
تذكى الروائح
البرد الشديد
ذبول التفاحة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Heat intensifying odors proves that smell is caused by evaporation of particles.
Correction: Evaporation may assist in conditioning the air, but the direct mechanism of smell is not necessarily evaporation; the observed phenomena do not logically require the evaporation theory.

Checkpoint
How does the author answer the evaporation proponents' objection that heat intensifies odors?
A) He admits that evaporation is the cause of smell.
B) He denies that heat intensifies odors.
C) He says that evaporation helps condition the air, but is not the direct mechanism of smell.
D) He argues that the apple wilting disproves the evaporation theory.

Key Takeaway
Observations that heat intensifies odors and apples wilt from sniffing do not prove the evaporation theory; they only show evaporation assists the process, not that it is the direct cause of smell.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you assumed that because one event consistently follows another, the first must be the cause. How might that assumption have led you to a wrong conclusion about your relationship with Allah or your understanding of His commands? ?
The objectors clung to observable phenomena to prove their theory, yet their conclusion was mistaken. Where in your life are you confidently using your own experiences or 'common sense' to decide what is true about Allah or the unseen, instead of submitting to what He has revealed? How can you surrender that area to Him starting now?
?
The apple was wilting, and they attributed it to the wrong cause. Is there a spiritual 'withering' in your life—a dryness in prayer, a lack of focus, a struggle with sin—that you have been blaming on the wrong thing? Objection from Distance and Its Answer
Al-Taftazani presents and rebuts the distance objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Taftazani refutes the objection that odors travel too far to be carried by conditioned air.

Core Idea
Distance does not disprove the medium; wind explains far-traveling odors.

The opponents raise a practical objection: if fire only heats a short distance, how can odor transform air over many days' journey? This seems to challenge the conditioned air theory. Al-Taftazani's answer clarifies that improbability is not impossibility, and the presence of strong winds can account for long-distance travel.

Guided Path
1
Read the Objection
Read the passage from 'وتمسك الآخرون' to 'مسيرة أيام' to understand the opponents' argument based on the fire analogy.
Note the use of Aristotle's report about vultures.
2
Analyze the Fire Analogy
Consider why the opponents compare odor to fire: both involve transformation of air, but fire only works at close range. Identify the logic.
3
Read the Answer
Read from 'والجواب' to the end. Notice the two parts: (1) it's only improbability, not proof; (2) wind can carry conditioned air.
4
Reflect on the Rebuttal
Ask yourself: Is the opponents' argument logically valid? How does the answer show a flaw in their analogy?
Consider that fire's effect is not the same as odor's effect; wind is a plausible explanation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتمسك الآخرون بأن النار مع شدة إحالتها لما يجاورها لا تسخن إلا مسافة قريبة منها، فكيف يحيل الجسم ذو الرائحة الهواء على مسافة بعيدة، ربما تبلغ مسيرة أيام على ما حكى أرسطو أنه وقع ملحمة ببلاد يونان، التي لا رخم فيها، فسافرت الرخم إليها لروايح الجيف من مسيرة أيام. والجواب: أنه استبعاد ولا دليل على الامتناع سلمنا، لكن وصول الهواء المتكيف إلى المسافات البعيدة على ما حكى يجوز أن يكون بهبوب رياح قوية.
And the others adhered to [the argument] that fire, despite its intense transformation of what is near it, does not heat except a short distance from it. So how can the odoriferous body transform the air at a far distance, perhaps reaching a journey of days, as it is related from Aristotle that a battle occurred in the land of Yunān, in which there were no vultures, so the vultures traveled to it for the smells of carcasses from a journey of days? And the answer: it is a mere improbability, and there is no proof of impossibility. Granted, but the arrival of the conditioned air to far distances according to what is related may be by the blowing of strong winds.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from another group (likely the proponents of direct action or others against the majority view) based on the apparent impossibility of odors traveling vast distances. They draw an analogy: fire, despite its intense power, only heats objects that are close to it. How then can an odoriferous body affect the air over distances of many days' journey? They cite Aristotle's report of a battle in Greece where vultures traveled from far away to smell the carcasses. The author answers by saying that this is merely a claim of improbability, not a proof of logical impossibility. Furthermore, he points out that even if the odor travels far, it is possible that the conditioned air is carried by strong winds. Thus, the argument from distance does not disprove the conditioned air theory; it only raises a difficulty that can be explained away.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If an odor travels far, the air cannot be the medium because direct influence is limited.
Correction: The distance argument is not a proof of impossibility; wind can transport conditioned air over long distances.

Checkpoint

A) He claims the distances are exaggerated.
B) He says that wind can carry the conditioned air over long distances.
C) He admits that the majority view is false.
D) He argues that fire can also heat far distances.

Key Takeaway
The objection from distance is not decisive; wind can carry conditioned air over long distances.

Philosophers on Celestial Smell and the Peripatetic Refutation
Al-Taftazani reports the philosophical debate on celestial smell and the Peripatetic refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Peripatetics denied smell to celestial bodies based on the absence of the necessary physical medium (air and vapor).

Core Idea
Celestial bodies cannot smell because there is no air or vapor in the heavens.

This lesson examines a philosophical debate about whether celestial bodies possess the sense of smell. Some ancient philosophers, including Plato and Pythagoras, claimed that the spheres and planets have odors. The Peripatetics (followers of Aristotle) refuted this by pointing out the absence of air and vapor in the celestial realm. Al-Taftazani reports this dispute in his commentary.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the English translation. Notice the flow of argument: claim, attribution, refutation.
The passage ends abruptly; focus on the complete argument.
2
Identify the two opposing views
Find the group that affirms celestial smell (Plato, Pythagoras, Hermes) and the refuting group (Peripatetics). Note their key points.
3
Understand the Peripatetic argument
The Peripatetics argue that smell requires air to be conditioned and vapor to evaporate. Since the celestial realm lacks both, smell is impossible there.
This is a physical/empirical argument, not metaphysical.
4
Reflect on the broader implication
Ask yourself: what does this debate tell us about how senses are understood in relation to different worlds (elemental vs. celestial)?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (ومن الفلاسفة) من يزعم أن للفلكيات شماً، وفيها روايح واشتراط وصول الهواء إلى الخيشوم، إنما هو في عالم العناصر. نقل عن أفلاطون وفيثاغورس وهرمس وغيرهم أن الأفلاك والكواكب لها شم، وفيها روائح. ورد عليهم المشاؤن بأنه لا هواء هنالك يتكيف، ولا بخار يتحلل. إنما هو في العنصريات، ومن كلمات بعض المتأخرين: أنا عند اتصالنا
He said: (And among the philosophers) is one who claims that celestial bodies have smell, and there are odors in them, and the condition of air reaching the nostril is only in the elemental world. It is transmitted from Plato, Pythagoras, Hermes, and others that the celestial spheres and planets have smell, and in them are odors. And the Peripatetics refuted them by [saying] that there is no air there to be conditioned, nor vapor to evaporate. It is only in the elemental world. And among the words of some later scholars: 'We, upon our connection...'
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage addresses the view of some philosophers who attribute the sense of smell to celestial bodies (the spheres and planets). The author quotes someone (likely the original text) stating that among philosophers, there are those who claim that celestial bodies have smell and that odors exist in them. However, the condition of air reaching the nostril is only applicable in the elemental world (the sublunar realm). He then reports that this view is attributed to Plato, Pythagoras, Hermes, and others. The Peripatetics (followers of Aristotle) refuted this by pointing out that in the celestial realm there is no air that can be conditioned, nor vapor that can evaporate – two necessities for smell. Thus, smell is confined to the elemental world. The passage ends with a fragment from some later scholars: 'We, upon our connection...' which is incomplete but suggests a continuation about human connection with the celestial world.

Correction: The Peripatetics argue that even if celestial bodies have souls, smell requires a physical medium (air and vapor) which does not exist in the celestial realm.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The Peripatetic refutation of celestial smell hinges on the absence of air and vapor in the heavens, showing that sensory faculties require a physical medium.

?

How will you correct that by distinguishing the domains?

The Physical Impossibility of Celestial Sounds
Al-Taftazani refutes celestial sounds using the condition of air as medium.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim of celestial sounds is physically impossible according to Aristotelian physics as used by Islamic theologians.

Core Idea
Sound requires air; the celestial spheres have no air, so no celestial sounds reach us.

Al-Taftazani begins his refutation by grounding the discussion in the physical conditions of sound. He argues that sound and hearing depend on air, a medium that does not exist in the celestial realm. Thus, the claim of hearing celestial sounds is inconsistent with the very principles that philosophers themselves accept.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition for sound
Read the passage and note that the occurrence of sound and hearing are conditioned by air (الهواء). This is a premise from Aristotelian physics.
2
Apply to celestial spheres
Since the celestial spheres are composed of a fifth element (ether) and have no air, they cannot produce sound. Moreover, even if they did, the sound could not penetrate the celestial body to reach us.
The impossibility of penetration (النفوذ) is also a key point.
3
Understand the conclusion
Therefore, the claim of celestial sounds is inconsistent with philosophical principles. The refutation is based entirely on the absence of a medium.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (فما يحكى) من سماع الأصوات الفلكية لا يستقيم على الأصول الفلسفية. يعني إن كان حدوث الصوت وسماعه مشروطين بالهواء، لم يكن التماس الأفلاك صوت، ولو فرض لم يمكن وصوله إلينا لامتناع النفوذ في جرم الفلك، لكن نسب إلى القدماء من الأساطين، أنهم يثبتون للفلكيات، أصوات عجيبة، ونغمات غريبة، يتحير من سماعها العقل، وتتعجب منها النفس، وحكى عن فيثاغورس، أنه عرج بنفسه إلى العالم العلوي، فسمع بصفاء جوهر نفسه، وذكاء قلبه، ونغمات الأفلاك، وأصوات حركات الكواكب، ثم رجع إلى استعمال القوى البدنية، ورتب عليها الألحان والنغمات وكمل علم الموسيقى.
He said: "As for what is narrated" about hearing celestial sounds, it is not consistent with philosophical principles. He means: if the occurrence of sound and its hearing are conditioned by air, then the spheres are not in contact [so they produce no sound], and even if it were supposed, it could not reach us due to impossibility of penetration through the celestial body. But it is attributed to the ancient masters that they affirm for the celestial bodies wonderful sounds and strange melodies, which reason is bewildered to hear and the soul marvels at. And it is narrated about Pythagoras that he ascended by himself to the upper world and heard, by the purity of his own essence and the sharpness of his heart, the melodies of the spheres and the sounds of the motions of the planets, then he returned to the use of bodily powers and arranged the musical melodies and tones based on that and perfected the science of music.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage refutes the philosophical claim that celestial bodies produce sounds that can be heard. The author states that such a claim is inconsistent with philosophical principles because sound and hearing are conditioned by the presence of air. Since the celestial spheres are composed of a fifth element (ether) and do not have air, they cannot produce sound. Moreover, even if they did, the sound could not penetrate the celestial body to reach us. The author then mentions that it is attributed to ancient philosophers, including Pythagoras, that they affirm wonderful sounds of the spheres. The anecdote about Pythagoras is given, that he ascended to the upper world and heard the melodies of the spheres by his pure soul, then returned to invent music theory. However, the author’s intent is to show that this is not consistent with the physical principles of sound, as the 'story' is not taken as a literal, physical phenomenon but perhaps as a spiritual or metaphorical experience. The refutation is based on the Aristotelian physics that sound requires a medium of air.

Key Terms
الأصوات الفلكية
الهواء
النفوذ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the philosophers' claim of celestial sounds is accepted in kalam, but this passage shows it is refuted based on the absence of air as a medium.
Correction: The refutation is based on the physical impossibility, not on rejection of the claim's source.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the claim of celestial sounds incompatible with philosophical principles?
A) Because the celestial bodies are made of fire and would burn the sound.
B) Because sound requires air, and the celestial spheres have no air.
C) Because the human ear is too weak to hear such high frequencies.
D) Because the philosophers never actually made such a claim.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of celestial sounds is proven by the absence of air, a necessary medium for sound.

The Pythagorean Claim of Celestial Melodies
Al-Taftazani recounts Pythagorean claim as non-literal spiritual experience.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the historical attribution of celestial sounds to Pythagoras and how al-Taftazani contextualizes it as non-literal.

Core Idea
Pythagoras' experience of celestial melodies is spiritual, not physical.

After presenting the physical refutation, al-Taftazani turns to the historical record. He notes that ancient sages, including Pythagoras, claimed to hear wonderful sounds from the celestial bodies. However, Pythagoras' ascent is described as a purification of the soul, not a physical journey. This suggests the sounds are metaphorical or spiritual, not literal physical sounds.

Guided Path
1
Read the anecdote
Read about Pythagoras ascending to the upper world and hearing the melodies of the spheres by the purity of his soul and sharpness of his heart.
2
Interpret the description
Note that Pythagoras heard 'by the purity of his own essence and the sharpness of his heart', implying a spiritual rather than physical hearing.
3
Connect to the refutation
Even though the ancients claimed such sounds, al-Taftazani's earlier refutation shows that physical sounds from the spheres are impossible. Thus, Pythagoras' experience must be understood as a spiritual vision, not a literal auditory phenomenon.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (فما يحكى) من سماع الأصوات الفلكية لا يستقيم على الأصول الفلسفية. يعني إن كان حدوث الصوت وسماعه مشروطين بالهواء، لم يكن التماس الأفلاك صوت، ولو فرض لم يمكن وصوله إلينا لامتناع النفوذ في جرم الفلك، لكن نسب إلى القدماء من الأساطين، أنهم يثبتون للفلكيات، أصوات عجيبة، ونغمات غريبة، يتحير من سماعها العقل، وتتعجب منها النفس، وحكى عن فيثاغورس، أنه عرج بنفسه إلى العالم العلوي، فسمع بصفاء جوهر نفسه، وذكاء قلبه، ونغمات الأفلاك، وأصوات حركات الكواكب، ثم رجع إلى استعمال القوى البدنية، ورتب عليها الألحان والنغمات وكمل علم الموسيقى.
He said: "As for what is narrated" about hearing celestial sounds, it is not consistent with philosophical principles. He means: if the occurrence of sound and its hearing are conditioned by air, then the spheres are not in contact [so they produce no sound], and even if it were supposed, it could not reach us due to impossibility of penetration through the celestial body. But it is attributed to the ancient masters that they affirm for the celestial bodies wonderful sounds and strange melodies, which reason is bewildered to hear and the soul marvels at. And it is narrated about Pythagoras that he ascended by himself to the upper world and heard, by the purity of his own essence and the sharpness of his heart, the melodies of the spheres and the sounds of the motions of the planets, then he returned to the use of bodily powers and arranged the musical melodies and tones based on that and perfected the science of music.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage refutes the philosophical claim that celestial bodies produce sounds that can be heard. The author states that such a claim is inconsistent with philosophical principles because sound and hearing are conditioned by the presence of air. Since the celestial spheres are composed of a fifth element (ether) and do not have air, they cannot produce sound. Moreover, even if they did, the sound could not penetrate the celestial body to reach us. The author then mentions that it is attributed to ancient philosophers, including Pythagoras, that they affirm wonderful sounds of the spheres. The anecdote about Pythagoras is given, that he ascended to the upper world and heard the melodies of the spheres by his pure soul, then returned to invent music theory. However, the author’s intent is to show that this is not consistent with the physical principles of sound, as the 'story' is not taken as a literal, physical phenomenon but perhaps as a spiritual or metaphorical experience. The refutation is based on the Aristotelian physics that sound requires a medium of air.

فيثاغورس
النغمات الفلكية
الأصوات الفلكية

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that Pythagoras literally heard physical sounds from the spheres, but the passage indicates it was a spiritual experience.
Correction: The description uses spiritual language ('purity of essence', 'sharpness of heart'), suggesting a non-physical hearing.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how did Pythagoras hear the celestial melodies?
A) Through his ears physically
B) By the purity of his soul and sharpness of his heart
C) By using musical instruments
D) By descending to the underworld

Key Takeaway
Pythagoras' hearing of celestial melodies is presented as a spiritual experience, not a physical one, consistent with the physical impossibility of cosmic sounds.

?
