The Role of 'Possessing Life Potentially' in the Definition
The author justifies the inclusion of 'possessing life potentially' and explains its benefit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the qualifier 'dhī ḥayātin bi-l-quwwa' is necessary and what it excludes.

Core Idea
This qualifier ensures the definition applies to all living souls, not just human, and guards against a specific view on celestial souls.

The author addresses a potential objection: why include 'possessing life potentially' when 'organic' might suffice? He explains that this qualifier prevents the definition from being limited to human souls and also serves to exclude the celestial soul according to one interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read how the author responds to the objection that 'possessing life potentially' is redundant.
The objection is that 'organic' already implies life.
2
Understand the benefit
Learn that this qualifier ensures the definition includes vegetative and animal souls, not just human.
3
See the exclusion of celestial souls
The qualifier guards against the celestial soul according to the view that the universal sphere has one soul and smaller spheres are its instruments.
Those who hold each sphere has its own soul do not need this qualifier.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن بعد ما لم يكن وإما بالنظر إلى ذات الجسم فكمال ثان والمراد بالجسم ههنا الجنس. أعني. المأخوذ لا بشرط أن يكون وحده أو لا وحده، بل مع تجويز يقارنه غيره، أو لا يقارنه لأنها الطبيعة الجنسية الناقصة، التي إنما تتم وتكمل نوعاً بانضمام الفصل إليه لا المأخوذ بشرط أن يكون وحده، لأنها مادة متقدمة بالوجود على النوع غير محمولة عليه، والنفس بالنسبة إليه صورة لا كمال يجعله نوعاً بالفعل، وقد سبق تحقيقها ذلك في بحث الماهية، وإنما أخذ الجسم في تعريف النفس لأنه اسم لمفهوم إضافي هو مبدأ صدور أفاعيل الحياة عن الجسم من غير نظر إلى كونه جوهراً أو عرضاً مجرداً أو مادياً، فلا بد من أخذه في تعريف النفس، لا من حيث ذاتها، بل من حيث تلك العلاقة لها كالبناء في تعريف الباني، والمراد بالطبيعي، ما يقابل الصناعي، وبالآلي ما يكون له قوى، وآلات مثل الغاذية والنامية ونحو ذلك، فخرج بالقيود السابقة الكمالات الثانية، وكمالات المجردات والأعراض وهيئات المركبات الصناعية، وبالآلي صور البسائط والمعدنيات. إذ ليس فعلها بالآلات لا يقال قيد ذي حياة بالقوة مغن عن ذلك. لأنا نقول. ليس معناه أن يكون ذلك الجسم حياً، ولا أن يصدر عنه أفعال الحياة، وإلا لم يصدق التعريف إلا على النفس الإنسانية، دون النباتية والحيوانية، بل أن يكون بحيث يمكن أن يصدر عنه بعض أفعال الأحياء، وإن لم يتوقف على الحياة، ولا خفاء في أن البسائط والمعدنيات كذلك. وفائدة هذا القيد الاحتراز عن النفس السماوية عند من يرى أن النفس إنما هي للفلك الكلي، وإن ما فيه من الكواكب والأفلاك الجزئية بمنزلة آلات له، فتكون جسماً آلياً، إلا أن ما يصدر عنه من التعقلات والحركات الإرادية، التي هي من أفاعيل الحياة تكون دائماً، وبالفعل لا كأفاعيل النبات والحيوان من التغذية والتنمية وتوليد المثل، والإدراك، والحركة الإرادية والنطق. أعني تعقل الكليات، فإنها ليست دائمة، بل قد تكون بالقوة، وأما عند من يرى أن لكل كرة نفساً، وأنها ليست من الأجسام الآلية، فلا حاجة إلى هذا القيد، ولهذا لم يذكره الأكثرون. وذهب أبو البركات إلى أنه إنما يذكر عوض قولهم: آلي فيقال: كمال أول طبيعي لجسم ذي حياة بالقوة وعبارة القدماء: كمال أول طبيعي لجسم آلي، واحترز بطبيعي عن الكمالات الصناعية، كالتشكيلات الحاصلة بفعل الإنسان. ثم قال: وقد يقال كمال أول لجسم طبيعي آلي بتأخير طبيعي، وهو إما غلط في النقل، وإما مقصود به المعنى الذي ذكرنا. فظهر أن ما يقال من أن بعضهم رفع طبيعي صفة لكمال، ليس معناه أنه يرفع مع التأخير صفة لكمال، ويخفض بعده آلي صفة لجسم، فإنه في غاية القبح، وكذا لو رفع حينئذ آلي أيضاً، صفة مع ذكر ذي حياة صفة لجسم، بل معناه أنه يقدم فيرفع على ما قال الإمام أن بعضهم جعل الطبيعي صفة للكمال فقال: كمال أول طبيعي لجسم أول آلي.
That after it was not, or with respect to the essence of the body, then a second perfection; and what is meant by 'body' here is the genus, meaning [the body] taken not conditionally that it is by itself or not by itself, but with the possibility that something else accompanies it or does not accompany it, because it is the deficient generic nature, which is only completed and perfected as a species by the attachment of the differentia to it, not [the body] taken conditionally that it is by itself, because it is matter prior in existence to the species, not predicated upon it; and the soul in relation to it is a form, not a perfection that makes it an actual species; and this has already been investigated in the discussion of quiddity. And the body is only taken in the definition of the soul because it is a name for an additional concept, which is the principle of the issuance of vital actions from the body without consideration of whether it is a substance or an accident, abstract or material; so it must be taken in the definition of the soul, not with respect to its essence but with respect to that relation, like 'builder' in the definition of 'builder'. And what is meant by 'natural' is what is opposed to artificial; and by 'organic' is what has powers and instruments, such as the nutritive, the growth, and so on. So by the previous restrictions are excluded the second perfections, the perfections of abstract entities and accidents and the dispositions of artificial compounds; and by 'organic' are excluded the forms of simple bodies and minerals, since their action is not by instruments. It may not be said: the qualification 'possessing life potentially' is sufficient instead of that. For we say: it does not mean that the body is living, nor that it issues vital actions; otherwise the definition would only apply to the human soul, not to the vegetative and animal; rather it means that it is such that some vital actions can possibly issue from it, even if not dependent on life; and it is not hidden that simple bodies and minerals are not like that. And the benefit of this qualification is to guard against the celestial soul, according to the one who holds that the soul is only for the universal sphere, and that the planets and partial spheres are like instruments for it, so it is an organic body; except that what issues from it of intellections and voluntary movements, which are among vital actions, are perpetual and actual, not like the actions of plants and animals of nutrition, growth, reproduction, perception, voluntary movement, and speech. I mean intellection of universals, for they are not perpetual, but may be potential. But according to the one who holds that each sphere has a soul, and that they are not among organic bodies, then there is no need for this qualification; and for this reason most do not mention it. And Abu al-Barakat held that it is only mentioned instead of their saying 'organic', so it is said: first natural perfection for a body possessing life potentially; and the expression of the ancients: first natural perfection for an organic body. And he guarded by 'natural' against artificial perfections, like the formations resulting from human action. Then he said: It may be said 'first perfection for an organic natural body' with a delay of 'natural'; and this is either a mistake in transmission, or intended to mean that which we have mentioned. So it appears that what is said, that some made 'natural' an adjective of 'perfection', does not mean that he raises it with delay as an adjective of 'perfection', and lowers 'organic' afterwards as an adjective of 'body', for that is extremely ugly; likewise if he then also raises 'organic' as an adjective, with the mention of 'possessing life' as an adjective of 'body'; rather it means that he advances and raises it, according to what the Imam said: that some made 'natural' an adjective of 'perfection' and said: first natural perfection for a first organic body.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage defines the soul (nafs) with the definition 'first perfection of a natural organic body' (kamal awwal tabi'i li jism ali). The author first clarifies 'body' (jism): it means the genus, taken without condition (la bi shart), i.e., the generic nature that is completed by the differentia. The soul is related to it as form, not as a perfection that actualizes the species. The body is included because the soul is the principle of vital actions from a body, so the definition must refer to this relation, just as 'builder' is used in defining a builder. Then 'natural' (tabi'i) is defined as opposed to artificial, and 'organic' (ali) as having instruments like nutritive and growth powers. These qualifiers exclude second perfections, perfections of abstract substances, accidents, artificial forms (via 'natural'), and forms of simple bodies and minerals (via 'organic'). The author then explains the qualifier 'possessing life potentially' (dhi hayatin bi-l-quwwa): it does not mean the body is actually alive, but that it can issue vital actions; otherwise the definition would only fit humans. Its benefit is to exclude the celestial soul according to the view that the soul is only for the universal sphere and that partial spheres are its instruments. However, those who hold each sphere has its own soul do not need this qualifier. The author then discusses Abu al-Barakat's alternative phrasing and a grammatical point about word order, concluding that the intended meaning is clear.

Key Terms
ذي حياة بالقوة
نفس سماوية
فلك كلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The qualifier 'possessing life potentially' means the body must be actually living.
Correction: The text states it means the body can possibly issue vital actions, not that it is actually living.

Checkpoint
What is the benefit of the qualifier 'dhī ḥayātin bi-l-quwwa'?
A) To exclude artificial bodies
B) To exclude the celestial soul according to some views
C) To include simple bodies
D) To define the soul as a second perfection

Key Takeaway
The qualifier 'possessing life potentially' ensures the definition applies to all living beings and excludes the celestial soul under a specific cosmological view.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author notes that most scholars omit this qualifier. When you encounter a consensus, do you blindly follow it, or do you pause to understand the reasoning behind each element? How might intellectual laziness affect your understanding of your own soul?
?
If you had to define what makes something alive without using technical terms, would your definition exclude plants or animals? This lesson shows that a precise qualifier can include or exclude whole categories of souls. What does this teach you about the care needed when speaking about Allah's creation?
?
Consider the celestial soul view: some treat planets as mere instruments. Are there beings in your life you treat as tools rather than souls deserving recognition? How does this lesson call you to see the life in all things?

Alternative Phrasing and Grammatical Clarification
The author presents an alternative phrasing and clarifies a grammatical confusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the alternative phrasing of Abu al-Barakat and the correct interpretation of the word order.

Core Idea
A small change in word order can lead to confusion; the correct interpretation preserves the intended meaning.

The author discusses an alternative definition by Abu al-Barakat that replaces 'organic' with 'possessing life potentially' and then addresses a grammatical point about the order of adjectives. This clarifies that the essential meaning is unchanged.

Guided Path
1
Read Abu al-Barakat's phrasing
Read how Abu al-Barakat modifies the definition: 'first natural perfection for a body possessing life potentially'.
He replaces 'organic' with 'possessing life potentially'.
2
Understand the word order issue
Some transmitted the definition with 'natural' delayed: 'first perfection for an organic natural body'. The author explains this is either a mistake or has a different intention.
3
Learn the correct interpretation
The correct reading is that 'natural' qualifies 'perfection' not 'body', and the intended meaning is preserved.
The author refutes a misunderstanding of the grammar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن بعد ما لم يكن وإما بالنظر إلى ذات الجسم فكمال ثان والمراد بالجسم ههنا الجنس. أعني. المأخوذ لا بشرط أن يكون وحده أو لا وحده، بل مع تجويز يقارنه غيره، أو لا يقارنه لأنها الطبيعة الجنسية الناقصة، التي إنما تتم وتكمل نوعاً بانضمام الفصل إليه لا المأخوذ بشرط أن يكون وحده، لأنها مادة متقدمة بالوجود على النوع غير محمولة عليه، والنفس بالنسبة إليه صورة لا كمال يجعله نوعاً بالفعل، وقد سبق تحقيقها ذلك في بحث الماهية، وإنما أخذ الجسم في تعريف النفس لأنه اسم لمفهوم إضافي هو مبدأ صدور أفاعيل الحياة عن الجسم من غير نظر إلى كونه جوهراً أو عرضاً مجرداً أو مادياً، فلا بد من أخذه في تعريف النفس، لا من حيث النقل، وإما مقصود به المعنى الذي ذكرنا. فظهر أن ما يقال من أن بعضهم رفع طبيعي صفة لكمال، ليس معناه أنه يرفع مع التأخير صفة لكمال، ويخفض بعده آلي صفة لجسم، فإنه في غاية القبح، وكذا لو رفع حينئذ آلي أيضاً، صفة مع ذكر ذي حياة صفة لجسم، بل معناه أنه يقدم فيرفع على ما قال الإمام أن بعضهم جعل الطبيعي صفة للكمال فقال: كمال أول طبيعي لجسم أول آلي.
That after it was not, or with respect to the essence of the body, then a second perfection; and what is meant by 'body' here is the genus, meaning [the body] taken not conditionally that it is by itself or not by itself, but with the possibility that something else accompanies it or does not accompany it, because it is the deficient generic nature, which is only completed and perfected as a species by the attachment of the differentia to it, not [the body] taken conditionally that it is by itself, because it is matter prior in existence to the species, not predicated upon it; and the soul in relation to it is a form, not a perfection that makes it an actual species; and this has already been investigated in the discussion of quiddity. Objection and Answer: Does the Definition Include Celestial Souls?
The author responds to an objection that the definition includes celestial souls.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objector argues that the definition of the soul cannot be common to earthly and celestial souls, and how the author responds by affirming that each celestial sphere has a soul, making the definition univocal.

Core Idea
The soul's definition applies to both earth and heavens because each celestial sphere has a soul.

After defining the soul as 'first perfection for a natural organic body possessing life potentially', a potential objection arises: does this definition also cover celestial souls, and if so, is it a case of homonymy? The objector challenges the universality of the definition, but the author counters by stating the correct school that each celestial sphere has its own soul, thereby preserving the univocal application of the definition.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objector claims that the definition, despite its qualifier, still includes both terrestrial and celestial souls, but that 'soul' is used by mere homonymy, so no single definition can cover both. Read the objection carefully.
Notice the three tests the objector proposes to show the definition fails.
2
Analyze the objector's tests
The objector considers three ways to define soul: by action alone, by action with intention/will, or by diversity of actions. Each leads to either overinclusion or underinclusion. This demonstrates the problem.
3
Grasp the author's response
The author asserts that the objection is based on the correct school, which holds that each celestial sphere has a soul. Therefore, the definition applies univocally to both types of souls, and the problem of homonymy dissolves.
This response depends on accepting the madhhab that celestial spheres are ensouled.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: فعلى ما ذكر من أن قيد ذي حياة بالقوة لإخراج النفس السماوية يكون قولنا: كمال أول لجسم طبيعي آلي معنى شاملاً للأرضية والسماوية صالحاً لتعريفهما به. وقد صرحوا بأن إطلاق النفس عليهما بمحض اشتراك اللفظ. إذ الأولى باعتبار أفعال مختلفة، والثانية باعتبار فعل مستمر على نهج واحد، وأنه لا يتناولهما رسم واحد، إذ لو اقتصر على مبدائية فعل، ما دخلت صور البسائط والعنصريات، وإن اشترط القصد والإرادة خرجت النفس النباتية. وإن اعتبر اختلاف الأفعال خرجت الفلكية. قلنا: مبني هذا التصريح على المذهب الصحيح. وهو أن لكل فلك نفسا.
It may be said: Based on what was mentioned that the qualification 'possessing life potentially' is for excluding the celestial soul, our saying 'first perfection for a natural organic body' would be a meaning inclusive of both terrestrial and celestial [souls], suitable for defining both of them. And they have stated that the application of 'soul' to both is by mere homonymy, for the first is with respect to different actions and the second with respect to a continuous action on one pattern, and that no single definition encompasses them; for if one restricted [the definition] to the principle of action, the forms of simple bodies and elements would enter; and if one stipulated intention and will, the vegetative soul would exit; and if one considered the difference of actions, the celestial soul would exit. We say: This statement is based on the correct school, which is that each celestial sphere has a soul.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objector notes that the qualifier 'possessing life potentially' was introduced to exclude the celestial soul, but the resulting definition 'first perfection for a natural organic body' actually includes both terrestrial and celestial souls, making it a common definition. However, it is claimed that 'soul' is used for both by mere homonymy (ishrak al-lafz), not univocally, because terrestrial souls have various actions while the celestial soul has a single continuous action. Thus, no single definition (rasm) can cover both. The objector tests this: if one restricts to 'principle of action' (without further qualification), simple bodies and elements (which have no soul) would be included; if one adds 'intention and will', the vegetative soul (which lacks intention) is excluded; if one considers 'difference of actions', the celestial soul (with uniform action) is excluded. Therefore, the definition cannot be common. The author's answer is direct: this objection is built on the correct school (al-madhhab al-sahih), which holds that each celestial sphere has its own soul. Thus, the definition does apply to both terrestrial and celestial souls univocally, and the problem of homonymy does not arise on this view.

Key Terms
نفس سماوية
اشتراك اللفظ
رسم
فلك
مذهب صحيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the definition of the soul cannot be common to earthly and celestial souls.
Correction: The author shows that on the correct madhhab (each celestial sphere has a soul) the definition is univocal.

Checkpoint
According to the author's response, what is the correct school (al-madhhab al-sahih) regarding celestial souls?
A) Celestial spheres have no souls.
B) Each celestial sphere has a soul.
C) The soul of the universe is one.
D) Celestial souls are not organic.

Key Takeaway
The objection that the soul's definition cannot be common to terrestrial and celestial souls is resolved by affirming that each celestial sphere has a soul, making the definition univocal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a theological objection that seems to undermine a core belief, do you immediately dismiss it or do you examine its premises as the author does here? What would it mean for your faith if you always took the time to understand the objection and respond from the correct school of thought? What specific doubt in your own heart will you now resolve by applying this method?
?
The objection assumes the term 'soul' is used homonymously for earthly and celestial souls. Are there concepts in your understanding of Islam that you treat as merely metaphorical or different in kind when they might actually be univocal and deeper in meaning? How would your connection to the cosmos and your own soul change if you believed your soul shares the same reality as the souls of the heavens? Take a moment to pray for a soul that reflects the harmony of the celestial spheres.

Proof and Refutation: Soul's Intellect of Abstracts
Author states proof and then refutes its premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the argument for the soul's immateriality based on intellection of abstracts is proposed and then challenged by questioning its underlying premises.

Core Idea
The proof rests on premises the opponent need not accept.

This passage presents a key argument for the soul's immateriality: if the soul were a body, it could not intellect abstract things, universals, simples, or incompatibles. The author then systematically unpacks and challenges the premises that this argument relies on, showing that the proof is not decisive unless those premises are conceded. By examining each premise, we see the dialectical nature of kalam reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof statement
Read the first sentence identifying the four aspects of the argument. Notice the concise presentation.
The proof is stated as a conditional: if soul were body, it would not intellect these categories.
2
Understand the answer structure
The answer begins by stating that the proof is based on unadmitted premises. Then seven premises are listed, each introduced by 'منها' (among them).
The premises are numbered implicitly; follow their logical flow.
3
Examine each premise
Go through the seven premises one by one. Consider why the opponent might deny each. The author often poses a rhetorical question ('why is it not permissible?') to weaken the premise.
Note that the author does not necessarily endorse these premises; he shows they are not self-evident.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author ends by noting that some of these premises are well-established, implying the argument works for those who accept them.
This shows the nuanced nature of kalam arguments: their validity depends on agreed foundations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا الوجه من الاحتجاج يمكن أن يجعل وجوهاً أربعة : بأن يقال : لو كانت النفس جسماً، لما كانت عاقلة للمجردات أو للكليات، أو للبسائط ، أو للممانعات.
والجواب : أن هذا مبنی الاحتجاج على مقدمات غير مسلمة عند الخصم،
منها أن تعقل الشيء، يكون بحلول صورته في العاقل، لا بمجرد إضافة بين العاقل والمعقول .
ومنها : أن النفس لو لم تكن مجردة لكانت منقسمة، ولم لم يجز أن تكون جوهراً وضعياً غير منقسم، كالجزء الذي لا يتجزأ؟ .
ومنها : أن الشيء إذا كان مجرداً كانت صورته الإدراكية مجردة يمتنع حلولها في المادي، ولم لم يجز أن تكون حالة في جسم عاقل؟ لكنها إذا وجدت في الخارج، كانت ذلك الشيء المجرد.
ومنها: أن صورة الشيء إذا اختصت بوضع ومقدار وكيفية لحلولها في جسم كذلك، كان الشيء أيضاً مختص بذلك، ولم لم يجز أن يكون في ذاته غير مختص بشيء من الأوضاع ، والكيفيات والمقادير .
ومنها أن الشيء إذا لم يقبل الانقسام كانت صورته الحاصلة في العاقل كذلك، ولم لم يجز أن تكون منقسمة بانقسام المحل العاقل، مع كون الشيء غير منقسم لذاته، ولو الحلوله في منقسم .
ومنها : أن الشيئين إذا كانا بحيث يمتنع اجتماعهما في محل كالسواد والبياض كانت الصورتان الحاصلتان منهما في الجوهر العاقل كذلك . وقد سبق أن صورة الشيء قد تخالفه في كثير من الأحكام.
ومنها : أن اجتماعهما في العاقل لا يجوز أن يكون لقيام كل منهما بجزء منه .
ومنها: أن انقسام المحل، يستلزم انقسام الحال فيه لذاته، ليمتنع حلول البسيط في العاقل الجسماني، المنقسم البتة، بناء على نفي الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ ، ولا يخفي أن بعض هذه المقدمات مما قامت عليه الحجة .
And this aspect of the proof can be made into four aspects: by saying: if the soul were a body, it would not be intellective of abstract things, or of universals, or of simple things, or of incompatible things. The answer: that this proof is based on premises not conceded by the opponent, among them: that the intellection of a thing is by the occurrence of its form in the intellective subject, not by a mere relation between the intellective and the intelligible. And among them: that if the soul were not immaterial it would be divisible, and why is it not permissible for it to be a positional substance not divisible, like the indivisible atom? And among them: that if a thing is immaterial its perceptual form is immaterial, impossible to inhere in a material thing, and why is it not permissible for it to be in a intellective body? But when it exists in the external world, it is that immaterial thing. And among them: that if a thing's form is specified by position, magnitude, and quality due to its inherence in a body so specified, then the thing would also be specified by that, and why is it not permissible for it in its essence to be not specified by any positions, qualities, and magnitudes? And among them: that if a thing does not admit division, its form occurring in the intellective subject is likewise, and why is it not permissible for it to be divided by the division of the intellective substrate, while the thing is undivided in itself, due to its inherence in something divided? And among them: that if two things are such that they cannot coexist in a substrate, like blackness and whiteness, the two forms occurring from them in the intellective substance are likewise. And it has preceded that a thing's form may differ from it in many rulings. And among them: that their coexistence in the intellective subject is not permissible to be by each of them subsisting in a part of it. And among them: that the division of the substrate necessitates the division of what inheres in it in itself, so the inherence of the simple in the bodily intellective subject, which is necessarily divided, is impossible, based on the denial of the indivisible part. And it is not hidden that some of these premises are such that the proof has been established upon them.
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the soul's immateriality and then refutes the premises underlying that proof. The author first states that the argument (that if the soul were a body it could not intellect certain things) can be broken into four aspects: the soul would not intellect abstract things (al-mujarradāt), universals (al-kulliyyāt), simple things (al-basā'iṭ), or incompatible things (al-mumāni'āt). Then the author gives the answer: this argument rests on premises not conceded by the opponent. Seven such premises are listed, each introduced by 'منها' (among them). The first premise is that intellection occurs by the form of the thing occurring in the intellective subject, not by a mere relation. The second is that if the soul were not immaterial it would be divisible, but why can't it be an indivisible positional substance like the atom? The third is that if a thing is immaterial, its form is also immaterial and cannot inhere in a material thing, but why can't it be in an intellective body? The fourth is that if a form is specified by position etc. due to its substrate, the thing itself would also be specified, but why can't it be unspecified in itself? The fifth is that if a thing is indivisible, its form in the intellect is also indivisible, but why can't the form be divided by the division of the substrate? The sixth is that if two things cannot coexist in a substrate, their forms in the intellect also cannot, but the author notes that forms may differ from the things. The seventh is that the division of the substrate necessitates the division of what inheres, so a simple thing cannot inhere in a bodily intellective subject. The author concludes that some of these premises are such that the proof stands upon them, implying the argument is valid against those who accept them.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might assume the argument is a simple deductive proof that does not depend on any assumptions.
Correction: The author shows that the argument is only as strong as its underlying premises, which the opponent may not concede. The argument is not self-evident.

Checkpoint

B) Three
C) Four
D) Seven

Key Takeaway
The argument for the soul's immateriality from intellection is not automatically decisive; its force depends on whether one accepts certain premises about the nature of intellection and inherence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you present evidence for your faith, do you check whether your premises are shared by your listener? What would it mean to you if someone rejects the very foundation of your argument?
?
The author points out that many premises in the proof are not conceded. Have you ever held a belief without examining the assumptions it rests on? How can you ensure your convictions are built on solid ground?
?

The Soul's Self-Perception and Immateriality
A proof for the soul's immateriality from its unique attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that the soul is immaterial because it perceives itself and does not weaken when the body ages.

Core Idea
The soul's unique self-perception proves its immaterial nature.

The author presents a second proof for the soul's immateriality, focusing on two remarkable attributes: the soul's ability to perceive itself and its instruments, and its resistance to weakening despite bodily decline. These qualities, unique to the soul, lead to the conclusion that it must be a non-material substance. Let's examine the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully, noting the structure: a claim followed by two supporting aspects.
2
Identify the first aspect
Focus on the first reason: the soul perceives itself and its instruments, unlike bodily senses which require an intermediary.
Consider why self-perception is impossible for material powers.
3
Identify the second aspect
Focus on the second reason: the soul does not weaken with the body; in fact, intellection often improves with age.
Think about the example of an old person being more insightful.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Synthesize both aspects to see why the soul must be immaterial: only a non-material entity can have these attributes.
This proof relies on induction and analogy (استقراء وتمثيل).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[قال (الثاني) إنها تدرك ذاتها، وآلاتها، وإدراكاتها، ولا يلحقها بكثرة الأفعال، وضعف الأعضاء والآلات ضعف وكلال بل قوة وكمال، ولا شيء من القوى الجسمانية كذلك، ومرجعه إلى استقراء وتمثيل]. أي من الوجوه الاحتجاج على تجرد النفس، أنها متصفة بصفات لا توجد للماديات ، وكل ما هو كدلك يكون مجرداً بالضرورة. بیان الأول : أنها تدرك ذاتها وآلاتها ،وإدراكاتها، ولا يلحقها بكثرة الإدراكات وضعف القوى البدنية ضعف ،وكلال بل ربما تصير أقوى وأقدر على الإدراك، ولا شيء من القوى الجسمانية كذلك، وهذا يمكن أن يجعل وجوها : أحدها: أنها تدرك ذاتها ،وآلاتها ،وإدراكاتها والمدرك الجسماني ليس كذلك كالباصرة، والسامعة والوهم والخيال لانها إنما تعقل بتوسط آلة، ولا يمكن توسط الآلة بين الشيء وذاته وآلته وإدراكاته . وتانيها : أن النفس لا تضعف في التعقل عند ضعف البدن وأعضائه وقواه، بل تثبت علمه أو تزيد، فإن الإنسان في سن الانحطاط ، يكون أجود تعقلاً منه في سن
[He said (the second): that it perceives itself, its instruments, and its perceptions, and it is not affected by the multiplicity of actions and the weakness of limbs and instruments with weakness and exhaustion, but rather strength and perfection, and none of the bodily powers are like that, and its referent is to induction and analogy.] That is, among the aspects of proof for the immateriality of the soul is that it is characterized by attributes that are not found in material things, and everything that is like that is necessarily immaterial. Explanation of the first: that it perceives itself, its instruments, and its perceptions, and it is not affected by the multiplicity of perceptions and the weakness of bodily powers with weakness and exhaustion, but rather perhaps becomes stronger and more capable of perception, and none of the bodily powers are like that. And this can be made into aspects: one of them: that it perceives itself, its instruments, and its perceptions, and the bodily perceiver is not like that, such as sight, hearing, estimation, and imagination, because they only intellect through the mediation of an instrument, and the mediation of an instrument between a thing and itself and its instrument and its perceptions is impossible. And the second: that the soul does not weaken in intellection upon the weakening of the body, its parts, and its powers, but rather its knowledge persists or increases, for man in the age of decline is more excellent in intellection than in the age...
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage presents a second proof for the soul's immateriality, based on its attributes that are not found in material bodies. The author first quotes the original text: 'the second (proof) is that it perceives itself, its instruments, and its perceptions, and it is not affected by the multiplicity of actions and weakness of limbs with weakness and exhaustion, but rather strength and perfection, and none of the bodily powers are like that, and its basis is induction and analogy.' Then the commentary explains that this proof shows the soul is characterized by attributes not found in material things, so it must be immaterial. The explanation breaks this into two aspects: first, that the soul perceives itself, its instruments, and its perceptions, whereas bodily perceivers like sight, hearing, estimation, and imagination only perceive through instruments, and it is impossible for an instrument to mediate between a thing and itself. Second, the soul does not weaken in intellection when the body weakens; in fact, it may become stronger. For example, an old man (in the age of decline) is more excellent in intellection than in his youth. The passage ends mid-sentence at the window boundary.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that bodily powers like sight or imagination can perceive themselves.
Correction: Bodily powers always require an instrument (e.g., the eye) that is distinct from themselves; they cannot perceive their own essence. The soul, however, does not need such mediation, proving its immateriality.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The soul's ability to perceive itself and to strengthen with age proves it is not a material substance.

?
If the soul strengthens with age while the body decays, what are you doing to nourish your soul as your body inevitably grows older? Are you investing in what will last, or are you only caring for what will perish?

The Argument from Divisibility of Locus
Sanusi presents a brief argument from divisibility of locus against a simple soul dwelling in a body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that a simple, indivisible entity like the soul cannot dwell in a divisible, composite body.

Core Idea
A divisible body cannot be the dwelling place of a simple soul.

This lesson presents a concise argument from the division of the locus (the body) to the impossibility of a simple entity dwelling in it. The argument hinges on the principle that the division of the container necessitates the division of the contained. Understanding this reasoning is crucial for proving the immateriality of the soul.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once straight through, then read the literal translation. Let the logical flow register before analysis.
2
Identify the logical components
Find the premise (division of locus entails division of dwelling), the conclusion (simple cannot dwell in composite), and the qualification (based on rejection of indivisible part).
The rejection of atomism is a key underlying assumption.
3
Reflect on the argument's strength
Consider the author's remark that some premises are proven. Ask yourself why this argument matters for theology.
This argument supports the immateriality of the soul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها: أن انقسام المحل، يستلزم انقسام الحال فيه لذاته، ليمتنع حلول البسيط في العاقل الجسماني، المنقسم البتة، بناء على نفي الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ ، ولا يخفي أن بعض هذه المقدمات مما قامت عليه الحجة .
And among them: that the division of the locus necessitates the division of the accident in it essentially, so that the dwelling of the simple in the rational bodily (thing), which is absolutely divisible, is impossible, based on the negation of the indivisible part; and it is not hidden that some of these premises are supported by proof.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage presents a brief argument for the immateriality of the soul. The author states that if a locus (the body) is divisible, then any accident (like the soul) dwelling in it must also be divisible, because the division of the locus entails the division of the indwelling accident. Consequently, a simple, indivisible entity cannot dwell in a composite, divisible body. This argument relies on the rejection of the atomistic 'indivisible part' (al-juz' alladhi la yatajzza'). The author notes that some of these premises are well-established by evidence.

Key Terms
انقسام المحل
حلول البسيط
العاقل الجسماني
الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument only applies to physical accidents, not to the soul.
Correction: The argument explicitly targets the 'simple' (al-basit), which includes the soul, and applies to any indwelling entity regardless of its nature.

Checkpoint
What does the divisibility of the locus (body) entail for any accident dwelling in it?
A) The accident becomes indivisible.
B) The accident also becomes divisible.
C) The accident becomes simple.
D) The accident becomes material.

Key Takeaway
The divisibility of a body makes it impossible for a simple, indivisible soul to dwell in it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your body can be divided into parts (cells, limbs), yet your sense of 'I' remains one and undivided, does that not force you to admit that your true self cannot be your body? How does this affect the way you treat your body and your soul?
?
This argument shows that your soul cannot be squeezed into a physical part of you. So where does your consciousness reside? Is it not humbling to realize that you are not a thing that occupies space, but something far more mysterious? How does that insight change your relationship with Allah, who alone knows the reality of your soul?
?
If the soul is simple and indivisible, it cannot die by being broken apart. Does that not give you a profound hope that your soul is immortal? What would you do today if you truly believed your soul will live forever beyond this body?

Three Subproofs for the Soul's Immateriality
Three subproofs for soul's immateriality based on self-perception, endurance, and non-weakening.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three subproofs for the soul's immateriality based on its ability to perceive itself, its instruments, and its perceptions without weakening.

Core Idea
The soul's unique ability to perceive itself proves it is not material.

The second argument for the soul's immateriality presents three inductive subproofs that highlight the soul's unique characteristics. These subproofs argue that the soul perceives itself, its instruments, and its perceptions, and unlike bodily powers, it is not weakened by age or activity. Let's read the passage and understand each subproof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice how the author introduces the three subproofs as 'wujuh' (aspects).
2
Identify the three subproofs
Find the three distinct arguments: (1) self-perception, (2) no weakening with age, (3) no weakening with repeated actions. Label them in your mind.
Each subproof is introduced by a signal word: 'Ahaduha' (one of them), 'Taniyaha' (the second), 'Thalithuha' (the third).
3
Analyze the reasoning of each
For each subproof, ask: Why does this show the soul is immaterial? What bodily power behaves differently? Write down your understanding before reading the commentary.
The third subproof involves the concept of 'infi'al' (passion) which is key to understanding bodily powers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (الثاني) إنها تدرك ذاتها، وآلاتها، وإدراكاتها، ولا يلحقها بكثرة الأفعال، وضعف الأعضاء والآلات ضعف وكلال بل قوة وكمال، ولا شيء من القوى الجسمانية كذلك، ومرجعه إلى استقراء وتمثيل. أي من الوجوه الاحتجاج على تجرد النفس، أنها متصفة بصفات لا توجد للماديات، وكل ما هو كذلك يكون مجرداً بالضرورة. بيان الأول: أنها تدرك ذاتها وآلاتها، وإدراكاتها، ولا يلحقها بكثرة الإدراكات وضعف القوى البدنية ضعف، وكلال بل ربما تصير أقوى وأقدر على الإدراك، ولا شيء من القوى الجسمانية كذلك، وهذا يمكن أن يجعل وجوها: أحدها: أنها تدرك ذاتها، وآلاتها، وإدراكاتها والمدرك الجسماني ليس كذلك كالباصرة، والسامعة والوهم والخيال لأنها إنما تعقل بتوسط آلة، ولا يمكن توسط الآلة بين الشيء وذاته وآلته وإدراكاته. وتانيها: أن النفس لا تضعف في التعقل عند ضعف البدن وأعضائه وقواه، بل تثبت علمه أو تزيد، فإن الإنسان في سن الانحطاط، يكون أجود تعقلاً منه في سن النمو، لما حصل له من التمرن على الإدراكات واستحضار صور المدركات، وكذا عند توالي الأفكار المؤدية إلى العلوم، مع ضعف الدماغ بكثرة الحركات وعند كسر سورة القوى البدنية بالرياضيات. فلو كان تعقلها بآلات بدنية لكانت تابعة لها في الضعف والكلال. وثالثها: أنها لو كانت من الماديات، لوهنت بكثرة الأفعال والحركات، لأن ذلك شأن القوى الجسمانية، بحكم التجربة والقياس أيضاً فإن صدور الأفعال عن القوى الجسمانية لا يكون إلا مع انفعال لموضوعاتها، كتأثر الحواس عن المحسوسات في المدركة، وكتحرك الأعضاء عند تحريك غيرها في المحركة، والانفعال لا يكون إلا عن قاسر يقهر طبيعة المنفعل، ويمنعه عن المقاومة فيوهنه، وهم معترفون بأن الوجوه الثلاثة إقناعية لا برهانية لجواز أن تدرك بعض الجسمانيات ذاتها وإدراكاتها من غير توسط آلة، وكذا لما هو آلة لها في سائر الإدراكات، وأن يكون كمال القوة الجسمانية العاقلة يتعلق بقدر من الصحة والمزاج يبقى مع البدن، أو بعضو لا يلحقه الاختلال، أو يتأخر اختلاله، وأن يكون حالها بخلاف حال سائر القوى في الكلال والانفعال.
He said (the second): That it perceives its essence, its instruments, and its perceptions, and is not affected by multitude of actions and weakness of limbs and instruments with weakness and fatigue, but rather strength and perfection; and none of the bodily powers are like that. Its reference is to induction and analogy. That is, from the aspects of argumentation for the immateriality of the soul: that it is characterized by attributes not found in material things, and everything that is such is necessarily immaterial. Explanation of the first: That it perceives its essence, its instruments, and its perceptions, and is not affected by multitude of perceptions and weakness of bodily powers with weakness and fatigue; rather, it may become stronger and more capable of perception; and none of the bodily powers are like that. This can be made into aspects: One of them: That it perceives its essence, its instruments, and its perceptions, and the bodily perceiver is not like that, such as the sight, hearing, estimation, and imagination, because they only apprehend through mediation of an instrument, and the mediation of an instrument between a thing and its essence and its instrument and its perceptions is impossible. The second: That the soul does not weaken in intellection with the weakening of the body, its organs, and its powers; rather, it confirms its knowledge or increases. For man in the age of decline is more excellent in intellection than in the age of growth, because of the training he has obtained in perceptions and the bringing to mind of forms of perceived things, and likewise with the succession of thoughts leading to sciences, despite the weakness of the brain due to many movements, and upon breaking the force of bodily powers through ascetic exercises. For if its intellection were through bodily instruments, it would be dependent on them in weakness and fatigue. The third: That if it were of material things, it would be weakened by multitude of actions and movements, because that is the characteristic of bodily powers, by experience and analogy as well. Indeed, the occurrence of actions from bodily powers is only with passion in their subjects, such as the effect of the senses from sensibles in the perceptive (powers), and such as the movement of limbs when something else moves them in the motive (powers); and passion is only from a compeller that subdues the nature of the passive being and prevents it from resistance, thus weakening it. And they acknowledge that the three aspects are persuasive, not demonstrative, because it is possible that some bodily things perceive their essences and perceptions without the mediation of an instrument, and likewise what is an instrument for them in other perceptions; and that the perfection of the bodily rational power is connected with a certain amount of health and temperament that remains with the body, or with an organ that is not subject to disorder, or whose disorder is delayed; and that its state is contrary to the state of other powers in fatigue and passion.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument for the immateriality of the soul, comprising three subproofs (wujuh), followed by an acknowledgment that these proofs are merely persuasive (iqna'iyya) and not demonstrative (burhaniyya). The author begins by quoting the argument: the soul perceives itself, its instruments, and its perceptions, and is not weakened by multiple actions or weakness of bodily organs; rather, it gains strength. No bodily power does this. The explanation then details three aspects: First, the soul perceives itself and its instruments, whereas bodily perceivers (like sight) require an instrument and cannot perceive themselves without mediation. Second, the soul's intellection does not weaken with bodily decline; it may even improve, as seen in elderly people who become wiser. If intellection depended on bodily instruments, it would weaken with them. Third, if the soul were material, repeated actions would weaken it, as happens with bodily powers due to passion (infi'al). The author then notes that scholars acknowledge these three aspects are only persuasive, because it is possible that some bodily things perceive themselves without an instrument, or that the rational power's perfection depends on a special organ or health that persists, differing from other powers. Thus the proofs are not conclusive.

Key Terms
تجرد النفس
قوى جسمانية
انفعال
إدراك الذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first subproof, why does the soul's perception of itself indicate its immateriality?
A) Because bodily perceivers like sight can also perceive themselves.
B) Because bodily perceivers require an instrument and cannot perceive themselves without mediation.
C) Because the soul is weaker than bodily powers.
D) Because the soul is made of a subtle material.

Key Takeaway
The soul's ability to perceive itself, its instruments, and its perceptions without weakening shows it is not material.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When your body weakens with age, do you find that your intellect and awareness can still grow? How does this personal experience affirm the immateriality of your soul? Commit to one way you will strengthen your soul's perception despite bodily weakness.
?
Consider your own moments of self-awareness: you perceive your thoughts and your own existence. How does this self-perception, as argued in the lesson, distinguish you from mere material things? Will you today spend a minute in silent awareness of your own soul as proof of your divine origin?
?
The lesson says the soul does not weaken with repeated actions but can strengthen. What spiritual practice can you engage in repeatedly that will strengthen your soul rather than weaken it? Make a pledge to do it this week.

Why These Proofs Are Merely Persuasive
Acknowledgment that the three proofs are persuasive due to possible counterexamples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the three subproofs for the soul's immateriality are considered persuasive rather than demonstrative, and the possible objections that prevent them from being conclusive.

Core Idea
Even strong inductive arguments can be undermined by counterpossibilities.

After presenting three subproofs for the soul's immateriality, the author acknowledges that these proofs are not demonstrative but only persuasive. He lists several possibilities that prevent these arguments from being logically conclusive. This lesson explores those reasons and their implications for kalam methodology.

Guided Path
1
Read the acknowledgment passage
Read the Arabic and translation of this short but important passage. The author explicitly states the status of the proofs.
The key term 'iqna'iyya' (persuasive) vs 'burhaniyya' (demonstrative) is central.
2
Identify the three possibilities
The passage lists three reasons why the proofs are not demonstrative. Identify each one: (1) some material things may perceive themselves without instrument, (2) the rational power's perfection may depend on special health/organ, (3) the soul's state may be different from other powers.
These possibilities are introduced by the phrase 'li-jawaz an' (because it is possible that).
3
Reflect on the significance
Think about why acknowledging these possibilities makes the argument persuasive rather than demonstrative. How does this affect the overall case for the soul's immateriality?
In kalam, distinguishing persuasive from demonstrative is crucial for rigorous theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (الثاني) إنها تدرك ذاتها، وآلاتها، وإدراكاتها، ولا يلحقها بكثرة الأفعال، وضعف الأعضاء والآلات ضعف وكلال بل قوة وكمال، ولا شيء من القوى الجسمانية كذلك، ومرجعه إلى استقراء وتمثيل. أي من الوجوه الاحتجاج على تجرد النفس، أنها متصفة بصفات لا توجد للماديات، وكل ما هو كذلك يكون مجرداً بالضرورة. بيان الأول: أنها تدرك ذاتها وآلاتها، وإدراكاتها، ولا يلحقها بكثرة الإدراكات وضعف القوى البدنية ضعف، وكلال بل ربما تصير أقوى وأقدر على الإدراك، ولا شيء من القوى الجسمانية كذلك، وهذا يمكن أن يجعل وجوها: أحدها: أنها تدرك ذاتها، وآلاتها، وإدراكاتها والمدرك الجسماني ليس كذلك كالباصرة، والسامعة والوهم والخيال لأنها إنما تعقل بتوسط آلة، ولا يمكن توسط الآلة بين الشيء وذاته وآلته وإدراكاته. وتانيها: أن النفس لا تضعف في التعقل عند ضعف البدن وأعضائه وقواه، بل تثبت علمه أو تزيد، فإن الإنسان في سن الانحطاط، يكون أجود تعقلاً منه في سن النمو، لما حصل له من التمرن على الإدراكات واستحضار صور المدركات، وكذا عند توالي الأفكار المؤدية إلى العلوم، مع ضعف الدماغ بكثرة الحركات وعند كسر سورة القوى البدنية بالرياضيات. فلو كان تعقلها بآلات بدنية لكانت تابعة لها في الضعف والكلال. وثالثها: أنها لو كانت من الماديات، لوهنت بكثرة الأفعال والحركات، لأن ذلك شأن القوى الجسمانية، بحكم التجربة والقياس أيضاً فإن صدور الأفعال عن القوى الجسمانية لا يكون إلا مع انفعال لموضوعاتها، كتأثر الحواس عن المحسوسات في المدركة، وكتحرك الأعضاء عند تحريك غيرها في المحركة، والانفعال لا يكون إلا عن قاسر يقهر طبيعة المنفعل، ويمنعه عن المقاومة فيوهنه، وهم معترفون بأن الوجوه الثلاثة إقناعية لا برهانية لجواز أن تدرك بعض الجسمانيات ذاتها وإدراكاتها من غير توسط آلة، وكذا لما هو آلة لها في سائر الإدراكات، وأن يكون كمال القوة الجسمانية العاقلة يتعلق بقدر من الصحة والمزاج يبقى مع البدن، أو بعضو لا يلحقه الاختلال، أو يتأخر اختلاله، وأن يكون حالها بخلاف حال سائر القوى في الكلال والانفعال.
He said (the second): That it perceives its essence, its instruments, and its perceptions, and is not affected by multitude of actions and weakness of limbs and instruments with weakness and fatigue, but rather strength and perfection; and none of the bodily powers are like that. ?

?

Third Argument: Continuous Intellection Impossible in a Body
Dichotomy argument for immateriality of soul

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument for the immateriality of the rational soul based on the impossibility of continuous intellection in a bodily substrate.

Core Idea
A material rational power could never think continuously or at all.

This passage presents a powerful argument that the rational power cannot reside in a body. The author forces a dilemma: if intellection depended on bodily presence, it would either never cease or never occur. Both options are absurd, so the soul must be immaterial. Let's examine the logic behind this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text slowly, noting the key terms like 'القوة العاقلة' and 'تعقل'. Then compare with the translation.
The passage is concise; every word carries weight.
2
Understand the dichotomy
Identify the two alternatives: either mere presence suffices for intellection (leading to continuous thought) or it does not (leading to impossibility).
The soul's self-knowledge is given as an example of continuous intellection.
3
Grasp the underlying principle
The argument rests on the idea that intellection requires acquiring a form, and multiple forms cannot coexist in one material substrate.
This principle is crucial for the impossibility of the second alternative.
4
Connect to the conclusion
Since both alternatives lead to absurdity if the rational power were material, the power must be immaterial (i.e., the soul is not a body).
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (الثالث): إن القوة العاقلة لو كانت في جسم، فإما أن يكفي في تعقله حضوره فلا ينقطع، كتعقل النفس ذاتها وصفاتها اللازمة، التي ليست بالمقايسة إلى شيء، أو لا فلا يحصل أصلاً، لامتناع تعدد الصور لشيء واحد في مادة واحدة، ومبناه على كون الإدراك بحصول الصورة.
He said (the third): If the rational power were in a body, then either its presence would suffice for its intellection, so it would not be interrupted, like the soul's intellection of its essence and its necessary attributes, which are not relative to anything; or it would not (suffice), so it would not be obtained at all, because of the impossibility of multiple forms for one thing in one matter. This is based on intellection being by the acquisition of the form.
Source pages p. 312

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument for the immateriality of the soul. The author states a dichotomy: if the rational power (al-quwwa al-'aqila) were located in a body, then either the mere presence of that body would be sufficient for that power to engage in intellection (in which case intellection would be continuous and never interrupted, like the soul's knowledge of itself and its essential attributes that are not relative), or it would not be sufficient, meaning that some condition must be satisfied for intellection to occur; but since multiple forms cannot coexist in a single material substrate, and intellection requires the acquisition of a form, it would be impossible for the rational power to ever obtain intellection if it were material. Therefore, the rational power cannot be material; it must be immaterial. The argument rests on the principle that intellection occurs through the acquisition of the form of the intelligible, and a single matter cannot hold multiple forms simultaneously, so a material rational power could not acquire successive intelligibles without contradiction.

Key Terms
القوة العاقلة
تعقل
تعدد الصور
مادة واحدة
حصول الصورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the dichotomy is incomplete, but the author argues that both alternatives lead to absurdity if the power is material.
Correction: The dichotomy is exhaustive: either presence suffices or it does not. Both are impossible for a material power, so the power must be immaterial.

Checkpoint
According to the third argument, if the rational power were in a body and its presence alone sufficed for intellection, what would be the result?
A) Intellection would be impossible.
B) Intellection would be continuous and never interrupted.
C) Intellection would weaken with bodily decay.
D) Intellection would require an instrument.

Key Takeaway
The rational power cannot be material because a material substrate cannot sustain continuous intellection or acquire multiple forms without contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your rational soul is immaterial and thus capable of continuous thought, why do you still find it so hard to maintain constant awareness of Allah? What worldly distractions are you allowing to disrupt your intellect?
?
The soul's knowledge of itself is uninterrupted. Is your self-awareness of your own soul constant, or do you often forget your own spiritual reality? What does that reveal about how much you value your inner self over your physical desires?
?
This argument proves that your rational power is not bound by matter. So why do you still let material concerns dominate your thinking? Are you willing to train your intellect to focus on eternal truths rather than fleeting pleasures?

Three Arguments for Transmigration and Their Refutation
Enumerates three arguments for transmigration and their refutations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three arguments used to defend metempsychosis (tanāsukh) and their refutations as presented by the author.

Core Idea
Transmigration's proofs are weak; each fails under scrutiny.

This passage systematically presents and dismantles the three main arguments for transmigration. The author first summarises the proponents' claims, then refutes each in turn. Understanding these refutations is essential for appreciating the Islamic theological stance on the soul and the afterlife.

Guided Path
1
Read the entire passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get an overview of the three arguments and their refutations.
Notice the structure: each argument is followed by 'و ردّ' implicitly.
2
Identify each argument
Locate the three arguments: the inactivity argument, the perfection argument, and the eternity argument. Note how each is introduced.
The arguments are numbered: الأول, الثاني, الثالث.
3
Understand each refutation
For each argument, identify the specific refutation given. The refutations either deny premises or provide counterpoints.
The refutation of the third argument is more complex, involving denial of eternal souls and finitude assumptions.
4
Reflect on the overall weakness
The author states 'الكل ضعيف' (all of this is weak). Consider why even plausible-sounding arguments fail in theological reasoning.
Transmigration is often a non-Muslim belief; these refutations reinforce Islamic doctrines of resurrection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (وغاية متشبثهم) : في إثبات التناسخ أنه لا معطل في الوجود وأن النفوس جبلت على الاستكمال، وذلك في التعلق، وأنها قديمة، فتكون متناهية، لاستنادها إلى علل وحيثيات متناهية لامتناع وجود ما لا يتناهى والأبدان غير متناهية، والكل ضعيف] . يعني ليس للتناسخية دليل يعتد به وغاية ما تمسكوا به في إثبات التناسخ على الإطلاق . أن النفس بعد المفارقة إلى جسم آخر إنساني أو غيره، وجوه . الاول : أنها لو لم تتعلق لكانت معطلة ولا معطل في الوجود، وكلتا المقدمتين ممنوعة . الثاني : أنها مجبولة على الاستكمال، والاستكمال لا يكون إلا بالتعلق ، لأن ذلك شأن النفس، وإلا كانت عقلاً لا نفساً . ورد بأنه ربما كان الشيء طالباً لكماله ، ولا يحصل لزوال الأسباب والآلات، بحيث لا يحصل لها البدل. الثالث : أنها قديمة لما سبق من الأدلة فتكون متناهية العدد لامتناع وجود ما لا يتناهى بالفعل، بخلاف ما لا يتناهى من الحوادث كالحركات والأوضاع، وما يستند إليها فإنها إنما تكون على سبيل التعاقب دون الاجتماع، والأبدان مطلقاً، بل الأبدان الإنسانية خاصة غير متناهية، لأنها من الحوادث المتعاقبة المستندة إلى ما لا يتناهى من الأدوار الفلكية وأوضاعها ، فلو لم تتعلق كل نفس إلا ببدن واحد، لزم توزع ما يتناهى على ما لا يتناهى، وهو محال بالضرورة. ورد بمنع قدم النفوس، ومنع لزوم تناهي القدماء لو ثبت فإن الأدلة إنما تمت فيما له ،وضع وترتيب وضع لا بتناهي الأبدان ،وعللها، ومنه لزوم أن يتعلق بكل بدن نفس، وإن أريد الأبدان التي صارت إنساناً بالفعل اقتصر على منع لا تناهيها .
He said: (And the utmost of their clinging) is in establishing transmigration: that there is no inactivity in existence, and that souls are instinctively disposed towards perfection, and that [perfection] is in attachment [to bodies], and that they are eternal, thus they are finite in number due to their dependence on finite causes and considerations, because it is impossible for the infinite to exist, while bodies are infinite. And all of this is weak. That is, the transmigrationists have no reliable proof, and the utmost they cling to in establishing transmigration absolutely is that the soul after separation [goes] to another human body or other [body]. [They have] aspects: First: that if it did not attach [to a body], it would be inactive, and there is no inactivity in existence. Both premises are denied. Second: that it is instinctively disposed towards perfection, and perfection can only be through attachment, because that is the nature of the soul; otherwise it would be an intellect, not a soul. It was refuted by that perhaps a thing seeks its perfection but does not obtain it due to the cessation of causes and instruments, such that no substitute obtains for it. Third: that souls are eternal, based on what preceded of proofs, thus they are finite in number because it is impossible for the infinite to exist in actuality, unlike the infinite of accidents like movements and states and what depends on them, for they occur only by succession, not simultaneously. And bodies absolutely, rather human bodies specifically, are infinite because they are from successive accidents dependent on infinite celestial cycles and their states. So if every soul attached only to one body, it would necessitate distributing what is finite over what is infinite, which is necessarily impossible. It was refuted by denying the eternity of souls, and denying the necessity of finitude of eternal things if established, for the proofs only hold for what has position and ordered position, not regarding the infinitude of bodies and their causes. And from it [follows] the necessity that every body has a soul attaching to it, and if what is intended is bodies that have actually become human, it [the reply] is restricted to denying their infinitude.
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the three arguments used by proponents of transmigration (tanāsukh). The author summarises that all these arguments are weak. The first argument claims that souls must attach to bodies to avoid inactivity, which would contradict the principle that nothing in existence is inactive. The refutation denies both premises: that souls would be inactive without attachment, and that inactivity exists. The second argument claims that souls are naturally inclined toward perfection, which can only be achieved through attachment to a body; otherwise they would be intellects. The refutation explains that a thing may seek perfection but fail to attain it due to loss of means. The third argument claims souls are eternal and therefore finite in number, while bodies are infinite (due to infinite celestial cycles), so souls must attach to multiple bodies to avoid distributing a finite number over an infinite number. The refutation denies the eternity of souls, and adds that even if souls were eternal, finitude does not necessarily follow unless ordered existence is assumed; moreover, bodies might not be infinite in the relevant sense.

Key Terms
تناسخ
نفس
تعطل
استكمال
تعلق
قديم
حدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Transmigration is necessary because souls naturally seek perfection through multiple bodily attachments.
Correction: The desire for perfection does not entail attainment; causes and instruments may be absent, preventing fulfillment.

Checkpoint
What is the refutation of the second argument for transmigration (that souls must attach to bodies to achieve perfection)?
A) It denies both premises of the first argument.
B) It argues that souls are eternal and finite, so they can only attach once.
C) It states that something may seek its perfection but not obtain it due to the loss of causes and instruments.
D) It argues that souls become intellects without attachment.

Key Takeaway
All three arguments for transmigration are weak and refuted; the soul's attachment to a body is not necessitated by these claims.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The first argument assumes the soul must always be active, but the refutation denies that inactivity is impossible. Are you afraid of stillness? Have you been filling your life with endless activity—work, entertainment, relationships—to avoid facing your soul's emptiness? Will you intentionally create space for silence and let your soul rest in the presence of Allah, even if it feels uncomfortable?
?
The second argument argues that seeking perfection guarantees obtaining it through attachment. But the refutation says desire does not guarantee attainment because means may cease. What 'perfection' are you chasing—a career, a relationship, a status—that you think will complete you? If that path were blocked, would your soul be lost? Will you instead seek your ultimate perfection in Allah alone, who never fails?
?
The third argument uses logic to demand that an eternal soul must transmigrate. But the refutation denies the soul's eternity and reminds you that this life is your only chance. Are you living as if you have unlimited opportunities? Do you put off repentance and good deeds, thinking you can make up later? Will you commit today to treat this life as your one and only opportunity to earn eternal success?

Philosophers on the Fate of Souls
Author presents philosophers' views on souls' post-death destinations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the threefold classification of souls after death according to some philosophers and the different realms they inhabit.

Core Idea
Souls are sorted into three destinies after death based on perfection.

Some philosophers proposed detailed views about what happens to souls after death, categorizing them by spiritual rank. Their scheme involves three distinct realms and a possible gradual purification. This passage presents that classification, which the author will later critique from an Islamic perspective.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get the overall sense of the philosophers' classification.
The passage is dense with technical terms like 'suspended similitude'.
2
Identify the three categories of souls
Find and list the three types of souls mentioned: perfect, intermediate, and wretched. Note their respective destinations.
3
Understand the two subcases within wretched souls
Notice that among the wretched, some remain in darkness forever while others can gradually transfer to the world of light. Ask why this difference exists.
The passage says it is according to their different ranks in wretchedness.
4
Reflect on the author's promise
The author ends by saying 'And you will know the meaning of the suspended similitude.' This signals that the concept will be clarified later, possibly for critique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجوز بعض الفلاسفة، فعلق النفوس المفارقة ببعض الأجرام السماوية للاستكمال وبعضهم على أن نفوس الكاملين تتصل بعالم المجردات، ونفوس المتوسطين تتلخص إلى عالم المثل المعلقة في مظاهر الأجرام العلوية على خلاف مراتبهم في ذلك، ونفوس الأشقياء إلى هذا العالم في ظاهر الظلمانيات والصور المستكرهة، بحسب اختلاف مراتبهم في الشقاوة، فيبقى بعضهم في تلك الظلمات أبداً، لكون الشقاوة في الغاية، وبعضهم ينتقل بالتدريج إلى عالم الأنوار المجردة، وستعرف معنى المثل المعلقة
And some philosophers permitted, so they attached the separated souls to some celestial bodies for completion, and some of them [held] that the souls of the perfect ones connect with the world of immaterial entities, and the souls of the intermediate ones become refined to the world of suspended similitude in the manifestations of the celestial bodies according to their different ranks in that, and the souls of the wretched ones [go] to this world in the image of darknesses and repulsive forms, according to their different ranks in wretchedness. So some of them remain in those darknesses forever, because the wretchedness is at the utmost, and some of them gradually transfer to the world of pure lights. And you will know the meaning of the suspended similitude.
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage presents the views of some philosophers regarding the fate of souls after death. They permitted that souls could attach to celestial bodies for completion. Then it explains a threefold classification: (1) Perfect souls connect with the world of immaterial entities; (2) Intermediate souls go to the world of suspended similitude in celestial bodies according to their rank; (3) Wretched souls enter this world in dark and repulsive forms. Some of the wretched remain in darkness forever due to extreme wretchedness, while others gradually move to the world of pure lights. The author promises to explain the meaning of 'suspended similitude' later.

عالم المجردات ('alam al-mujarradat)
عالم المثل المعلقة ('alam al-muthul al-mu'allaqa)
عالم الأنوار المجردة ('alam al-anwar al-mujarrada)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These philosophical views are part of Islamic eschatology.
Correction: The author presents these as the views of some philosophers (falāsifa), not as Islamic doctrine. They are mentioned likely for critique.

Checkpoint
According to some philosophers, what is the fate of the souls of the intermediate ones?
A) They connect with the world of immaterial entities.
B) They go to the world of suspended similitude in celestial bodies.
C) They remain in darkness forever.
D) They gradually transfer to the world of pure lights.

Key Takeaway
Philosophers classified souls after death into three categories: perfect (to immaterial world), intermediate (to suspended similitude), and wretched (to darkness, with possible gradual purification).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your soul were categorized today by these philosophers, would you be among the perfect, intermediate, or wretched? What evidence from your own life supports that—and does it frighten you enough to change?
?
The passage says some wretched souls gradually transfer to the world of pure lights. What 'darkness' in your character are you tolerating, assuming you can be purified later—when you could strive now to move toward the light?
?
You have just read a classification of souls based on perfection. Look at your last week: where have you chosen comfort over growth, or pride over humility? How will you respond to that specific failure today, not tomorrow?

Interpretations of the Intellect Stages Among Scholars
Taftazani introduces different verbal interpretations of the intellect stages.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different ways scholars define the terms for the intellect stages and the conceptual distinctions involved.

Core Idea
The intellect stages are not fixed terms; scholars vary in what they name: dispositions, soul, or faculties.

In this passage, Taftazani first compares the intellect to sunlight, then notes that scholars disagree on how to name the stages. He presents multiple interpretations for the material intellect and the intellect by disposition. This lesson clarifies these different verbal traditions and their conceptual bases.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening simile
Notice the analogy of the intellect to the sun. Taftazani uses this to highlight the intellect's illuminating role.
The simile is part of the broader discussion.
2
Identify the three interpretative approaches
The passage distinguishes whether terms refer to dispositions, the soul qualified by them, or faculties in the soul. Look for this triple distinction.
3
Examine the examples for material intellect
See how the material intellect is defined in three ways: as a disposition, a dispositional faculty, or the soul at its beginning.
The same pattern applies to the other stages.
4
Read the definition of intellect by disposition
Note the specific statement: it is the acquisition of necessary truths that lead to theoretical ones.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونسبته إلينا نسبة الشمس إلى أبصارنا، وتختلف عبارات القوم في أن المذكورات أسام لهذه الاستعدادات والكمال، أو للنفس باعتبار اتصافها بها، أو لقوى في النفس هي مباديها، مثلاً يقال تارة : إن العقل الهيولاني، هو استعداد النفس لقبول العلوم الضرورية، وتارة أنه قوة استعدادية، أو قوة من شأنها الاستعداد المحض، وتارة أنه النفس في مبدأ الفطرة من حيث قابليتها للعلوم، وكذا في البواقي وربما يقال إن العقل بالملكة هو حصول الضروريات من حيث تتأدى إلى النظريات. وقال ابن سينا هو صورة المعقولات الأولى، تتبعها القوة على كسب غيرها بمنزلة الضوء للأبصار والمستفاد هو المعقولات المكتسبة عند حصولها بالفعل. وقال في كتاب المبدأ والمعاد. إن العقل بالفعل، والعقل المستفاد واحد بالذات مختلف بالاعتبار، فإنه من جهة تحصيله للنظريات عقل بالفعل، ومن جهة حصولها فيه بالفعل عقل مستفاد.
And its relation to us is like the relation of the sun to our eyes. The expressions of the people differ as to whether the mentioned [terms] are names for these dispositions and perfections, or for the soul by virtue of its being qualified by them, or for faculties in the soul that are their principles. For example, it is sometimes said that the material intellect (al-'aql al-hayulani) is the soul's disposition for receiving necessary sciences; and sometimes that it is a dispositional faculty, or a faculty whose function is pure disposition; and sometimes that it is the soul at the beginning of its nature (fitra) in terms of its capacity for sciences. And likewise for the rest. And perhaps it is said that the intellect by disposition (al-'aql bi-l-malaka) is the acquisition of necessary truths inasmuch as they lead to theoretical truths. Ibn Sina said: it is the form of the first intelligibles, followed by the power to acquire others, like light for sight. And the acquired intellect (al-mustafad) is the acquired intelligibles when they are actually attained. And he said in the book al-Mabda' wa al-Ma'ad: The actual intellect (al-'aql bi-l-fi'l) and the acquired intellect (al-'aql al-mustafad) are one in essence but differ in consideration; for from the perspective of its acquisition of theoretical truths it is actual intellect, and from the perspective of their actual existence in it it is acquired intellect.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of the intellect states. The author begins by stating that the relation of the intellect to us is like the relation of the sun to our eyes, indicating that the intellect is a light by which we see. Then he notes that scholars differ in terminology: whether the terms 'material intellect', 'intellect by disposition', etc., are names for the dispositions and perfections themselves, or for the soul qualified by them, or for the faculties that are their sources. He then gives examples: the material intellect is sometimes defined as the soul's disposition for necessary sciences, sometimes as a dispositional faculty, sometimes as the soul at the beginning of its nature. Similarly, the intellect by disposition is said to be the acquisition of necessary truths insofar as they lead to theoretical ones. Then he quotes Ibn Sina: the intellect by disposition is the form of the first intelligibles, giving power to acquire others, like light for sight; and the acquired intellect is the acquired intelligibles when actual. He also notes that in al-Mabda' wa al-Ma'ad, Ibn Sina identifies the actual and acquired intellects as one in essence but different in consideration.

Key Terms
العقل الهيولاني
العقل بالملكة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the four intellects are separate substances with fixed definitions.
Correction: The passage shows that scholars differ in what they name; sometimes it's the disposition, sometimes the soul, etc.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the three possible referents for the intellect terms?
A) Dispositions, the soul, and faculties
B) The soul, the body, and the mind
C) The necessary sciences, the theoretical sciences, and the intellect
D) The material, the dispositional, the actual, and the acquired

Key Takeaway
The terms for the intellect stages are interpreted variably as referring to dispositions, the soul, or faculties.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter differing scholarly interpretations of a single concept, like the intellect stages, do you feel unsettled or enriched? How does your reaction reveal whether you prioritize certainty or depth of understanding, and what is at stake for your faith if you insist on one fixed meaning?
?
Reflect on a time you assumed a theological term had only one correct definition. How did that assumption limit your learning? Are you willing to hold multiple valid interpretations in your mind, even when they feel contradictory? If not, what fear drives that resistance?
?
This lesson shows that scholars often describe the same reality (the soul's capacity for knowledge) from different angles. Does your own spiritual life allow for such multiplicity—for example, seeing your faith as both a disposition and an active faculty? Or do you reduce it to one aspect? What might you gain by embracing the diversity of descriptions?
