Ibn Sina's Definitions of the Intellect Stages
Taftazani quotes Ibn Sina's definitions of intellect stages.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's specific definitions for the intellect by disposition, the acquired intellect, and his identification of the actual and acquired intellect.

Core Idea
Ibn Sina defines the intellect by disposition as the form of first intelligibles and identifies actual and acquired intellect as one in essence.

In this part of the text, Taftazani quotes Ibn Sina directly to provide his definitions. Ibn Sina explains the intellect by disposition as the form of first intelligibles that enable acquiring others, compares it to light, and then defines the acquired intellect as actually possessed intelligibles. He further clarifies in al-Mabda' wa al-Ma'ad that the actual and acquired intellect are the same in essence but viewed differently.

Guided Path
1
Read Ibn Sina's definition of intellect by disposition
Notice that Ibn Sina defines it as the form of the first intelligibles, followed by the power to acquire others. The metaphor of light is key.
2
Understand the acquired intellect
It is the acquired intelligibles when actually present in the soul.
This contrasts with the dispositional state.
3
Examine the identification in al-Mabda' wa al-Ma'ad
Ibn Sina says actual and acquired intellect are one in essence but differ in consideration (acquisition vs. presence).
This is a subtle but important point.

The Acquired Intellect: Constant Presence or Mere Possession?
Taftazani presents two interpretations of the acquired intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate on whether the acquired intellect demands constant presence of theoretical truths or just possession.

Core Idea
The acquired intellect is either angelic constancy or simple possession of truths.

Taftazani presents a key disagreement about the acquired intellect. Some scholars argue it requires perpetual awareness of all theoretical truths, reaching an angelic state; others say it is mere presence of truths. This passage explores both views and hints at the preferred position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and its translation. Notice the two interpretations of 'presence'.
The passage begins with a definition and then introduces the disagreement.
2
Identify the two views
Find where Taftazani presents the first view (constant presence) and the second view (mere presence). Observe how each relates to the body and angelic ranks.
The first view is associated with impossibility while in the body.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Taftazani hints that the second view aligns better with the agreed four ranks, but constant presence is the perfection of that rank. Consider why this distinction matters.
This connects to the learning objective about the nature of the acquired intellect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وربما قيل : هو عقل بالفعل بالقياس إلى ذاته ، ومستفاد بالقياس إلى فاعله . واختلفوا أيضاً في أن المعتبر في المستفاد هو حضور النظريات الممكنة للنفس، بحيث لا تغيب أصلاً حتى قالوا إنه آخر المراتب البشرية، وأول المنازل الملكية، وأنه يمتنع أو يستبعد جداً ما دامت النفس متعلقة بالبدن، أو مجرد الحضور حتى يكون قبل العقل بالفعل بحسب الوجود على ما صرح به الإمام، وإن كان بحسب الشرف هو الغاية، والرئيس المطلق الذي يخدمه سائر القوى من الإنسانية، والحيوانية، والنباتية، ولا يخفى أن هذا أشبه بما اتفقوا عليه من حصر المراتب في الأربع . نعم حضور الكل بحيث لا يغيب أصلاً، هو كمال مرتبة المستفاد .
And perhaps it is said: It is actual intellect relative to itself, and acquired relative to its agent. They also disagreed concerning what is considered in the acquired intellect: is it the presence of possible theoretical truths in the soul such that they never absent at all, so that they said it is the last of the human ranks and the first of the angelic stations, and that it is impossible or very unlikely as long as the soul is attached to the body? Or is it mere presence such that it even precedes the actual intellect in existence, as stated by the Imam, although in terms of honor it is the ultimate goal, and the absolute master which all other powers—human, animal, and plant—serve? It is not hidden that this is more similar to what they agreed upon of limiting the ranks to four. Yes, the presence of everything such that it never absents at all is the perfection of the rank of the acquired intellect.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage discusses the disagreement about the nature of the acquired intellect. Some say it is the constant presence of all theoretical truths in the soul, reaching an angelic level, but this is impossible or very difficult while the soul is in the body. Others say it is simply presence, which can occur before the actual intellect. The author favors the view that it is presence, and notes that constant presence is the perfection of that rank.

D) It is the same as the material intellect.

Key Takeaway
The nature of the acquired intellect is debated: either it requires constant presence of all theoretical truths (angelic) or mere presence, with the latter being more compatible with the four ranks of intellect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Taftazani describes the acquired intellect as the absolute master served by all other powers—human, animal, and plant. In your daily life, what actually serves what? Do your desires and habits serve your intellect, or has your intellect become a servant to your lower impulses? What must change for your intellect to truly rule?
?
The passage suggests that mere presence of truths is enough for the acquired intellect, but constant presence is perfection. Which truth about yourself have you been avoiding facing constantly? What step will you take today to bring that truth from mere possession to constant presence in your heart?

Al-Razi's Four States of the Soul: The Four Intellects
Taftazani presents al-Razi's classification of the soul's four states corresponding to the four intellects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's classification of the four states of the soul (the four intellects) and the critique of a definition found in al-Mawaqif.

Core Idea
The soul's intellect progresses from potential to actual presence.

This passage presents Imam al-Razi's detailed enumeration of the soul's four states, corresponding to the four intellects. Taftazani explains each state and then critiques a definition of actual intellect from al-Mawaqif, noting it is not found in earlier scholars.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the logical progression of the four states.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the four states
List the four states: material intellect, intellect by disposition, actual intellect, and acquired intellect. Understand the condition for each.
Al-Razi defines them in order of increasing actualization.
3
Examine the critique
Note Taftazani's criticism of the definition of actual intellect from al-Mawaqif. Compare it with al-Razi's definition.
This shows the importance of precise terminology in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذكر الإمام في بيان المراتب . أن النفس إن خلت عن العلوم مع أنها قابلة لها، سميت في فلك الحالة عقلاً هيولانياً، وإلا فإن حصلت الضروريات فقط سميت حينئذ عقلاً بالملكة، وإن حصلت النظريات أيضاً، فإن لم تكن حاصلة بالفعل، بل لها قوة الاستحضار بمجرد التوجه سميت النفس حينئذ عقلا بالفعل، وإن كانت النفس حاضرة سميت النفس عقلاً مستفاداً. فالحالات أربع لا غير حالة الخلو، وحالة حصول الضروريات وحالة حصول النظريات بدون الحضور وحالة حصولها مع الحضور والمراتب هذه النفس باعتبارها وهو موافق لما قال ابن سينا، ان النفس تكون عقلاً بالملكة، ثم عقلاً بالفعل، ثم عقلاً مستفاداً، والمعنى أن حالتها مستفادة ، وأما ما ذكر في المواقف من أن العقل بالفعل هو ملكة استنباط النظريات من الضروريات. أي ضرورة العقل، بحيث متى شاء استحضر الضروريات، واستنتج منها النظريات فلم نجده في كلام القوم .
And the Imam mentioned in the explanation of the ranks: that if the soul is devoid of sciences while being capable of them, it is called in that state a material intellect (hayulani). Otherwise, if only the necessary sciences are acquired, it is then called an intellect by disposition (bi-l-malaka). And if the theoretical sciences are also acquired, but not actually present, rather it has the power to recall them by mere attention, then the soul is called an actual intellect (bi-l-fi'l). And if the soul is present [with them], it is called an acquired intellect (mustafad). So the states are four only: a state of emptiness, a state of acquisition of necessary sciences, a state of acquisition of theoretical sciences without presence, and a state of their acquisition with presence. And these ranks are of the soul by virtue of itself. And this agrees with what Ibn Sina said: that the soul becomes intellect by disposition, then actual intellect, then acquired intellect; meaning that its state is acquired. As for what is mentioned in al-Mawaqif, that the actual intellect is the faculty of deducing theoretical truths from necessary truths—i.e., from the necessities of reason, such that whenever it wishes it recalls the necessary truths and deduces the theoretical ones from them—we did not find this in the words of the scholars.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage presents Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi's clear enumeration of the four states of the soul corresponding to the four intellects. The author explains each state: empty but capable, then with necessary sciences only (intellect by disposition), then with theoretical sciences but not constantly present (actual intellect), then with constant presence (acquired intellect). He then criticizes a definition from al-Mawaqif that defines actual intellect as a faculty of deduction, stating that this definition is not found in the earlier scholars' speech.

Key Terms
عقلاً هيولانياً
عقلاً بالملكة
عقلاً بالفعل
عقلاً مستفاداً
المواقف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the actual intellect is a faculty of deduction rather than a state of presence of theoretical truths.
Correction: Al-Razi and earlier scholars define actual intellect as the state where theoretical sciences are acquired but not constantly present, only recallable by attention. The definition from al-Mawaqif as a faculty of deduction is not found in the earlier authoritative texts.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi, what is the state of the soul called when it has acquired theoretical sciences but they are not actually present?
A) Material intellect
B) Intellect by disposition
C) Actual intellect
D) Acquired intellect

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi defines four states of the soul, with the highest being the acquired intellect where knowledge is constantly present.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of the four states best describes your current relationship with the knowledge of faith you have been taught? Is it merely present when you recall it, or do you live with it constantly present?
?
If the highest state is to have theoretical truths constantly present, what is one truth from this lesson that you are determined to keep before your mind throughout this day—and how will you remind yourself of it?
?
Al-Razi says the soul progresses from emptiness to constant presence. What is the one thing you are most afraid to face in your own soul that might be keeping you from that constant presence?

Definition of the Practical Intellect
Taftazani defines the practical intellect and explains its functions and reliance on the theoretical intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition and function of the practical intellect according to Taftazani, and its relationship with the theoretical intellect.

Core Idea
The practical intellect guides actions by deriving particular opinions from universal truths.

Taftazani begins by distinguishing the practical intellect from the theoretical. He defines it as the power that governs our actions, crafts, and moral discernment, and explains how it depends on the theoretical intellect for its particular judgments. This passage lays the foundation for understanding how human beings navigate both worldly and religious affairs through reasoned action.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation to grasp the flow of Taftazani's definition and explanation.
Notice the three functions: acting on subject matters, deriving crafts, and distinguishing good from corrupt.
2
Identify the key components
Break down the definition: (1) the practical intellect is a power, (2) it deals with subject matters like wood for a carpenter, (3) it derives crafts, (4) it distinguishes good from evil for ordering livelihood and afterlife. Also note its reliance on the theoretical intellect through particular opinions.
The phrase 'يستعين بالنظري' highlights its dependence.
3
Understand the summary and relation
The passage concludes that the practical intellect is the principle of motion for the human body towards particular deliberative acts, in line with suitable opinions, and relates to the appetitive faculty. Reflect on how this connects action to reason.
The text ends abruptly with 'ومنها', but the main point is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (وأما العملي) : وهو قوة التصرف في الموضوعات واستنباط الصناعات، وتمييز الصالح الفاسد لانتظام أمر المعاش والمعاد، فيستعين بالنظري من جهة أن أفاعيله تنبعث عن آراء جزئية مستنبطة عن الآراء الكلية . من يعني أنها قوة بها يتمكن الإنسان من استنباط الصناعات والتصرفات في موضوعاتها، التي هي بمنزلة المواد كالخشب للنجار، وتمييز مصالحه التي يجب الإتيان بها من المفاسد التي يجب الاجتناب عنها، لينتظم بذلك أمر معاشه، ومعاده . وبالجملة هي مبدأ حركة بدن الإنسان الى الأفاعيل الجزئية، الخاصة بالروية، على مقتضى آراء تخصها صلاحية ولها نسبة الى القوة النزوعية، ومنها
He said (As for the practical intellect): It is the power of acting upon subject matters, deriving crafts, and distinguishing good from corrupt for the ordering of livelihood and afterlife. It seeks help from the theoretical intellect in that its acts arise from particular opinions derived from universal opinions. [Commentary:] It means that it is a power by which human beings are able to derive crafts and actions in their subject matters, which are like materials—such as wood for the carpenter—and to distinguish his interests that must be performed from corruptions that must be avoided, so that his livelihood and afterlife are ordered. In summary, it is the principle of motion of the human body towards particular acts, specific to deliberation, in accordance with opinions pertaining to its suitability, and it has a relation to the appetitive faculty, and from it...
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage defines the practical intellect. It begins by quoting a definition: it is the power of acting on subject matters, deriving crafts, and distinguishing good from corrupt for the sake of ordering livelihood and afterlife. It depends on the theoretical intellect because its actions stem from particular opinions derived from universal ones. Then the author explains this: it is a power that enables humans to derive crafts and distinguish beneficial from harmful, thereby ordering worldly and religious affairs. It is the principle of bodily motions towards particular actions that are deliberative, in line with suitable opinions, and relates to the appetitive faculty.

Key Terms
العقل العملي
القوة النزوعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The practical intellect operates independently of the theoretical intellect.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that the practical intellect seeks help from the theoretical intellect, as its particular opinions are derived from universal ones.

Checkpoint
What does the practical intellect seek help from, according to the passage?
A) The appetitive faculty
B) The theoretical intellect
C) The material intellect
D) The acquired intellect

Key Takeaway
The practical intellect is the faculty that enables humans to manage their actions and distinguish good from evil by deriving particular opinions from the theoretical intellect, and it is the source of bodily motion towards deliberative acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How are you currently using your practical intellect to distinguish good from corrupt actions in your daily life? Are you consciously deriving particular opinions from the universal truths of revelation? Commit to one specific area where you will apply this process today.
?
The practical intellect orders both your livelihood and afterlife. Reflect: Is your pursuit of worldly affairs overshadowing your preparation for the afterlife? What is one concrete change you can make to rebalance your priorities starting now?
?
Your actions arise from particular opinions derived from universal ones. Which universal truth from the Quran or Sunnah have you neglected to apply in your decisions? What will you do differently tomorrow to let that truth guide your actions?

The Three Powers of the Soul
Sanusi introduces the three soul powers and summarizes the virtues and their extremes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three fundamental powers of the human soul that form the basis for the cardinal virtues.

Core Idea
Every virtue arises from moderating one of three soul powers.

Sanusi begins by identifying the three inner powers that drive all human actions: the concupiscent, irascible, and rational powers. Understanding these powers is essential before exploring the virtues that temper them. This passage lays the foundation for the rest of his ethical framework.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on soul powers
Read the Arabic text carefully, noting the three powers and their alternative names. The translation will help clarify the terms.
The concupiscent power is also called 'bestial' and 'commanding soul'; the irascible is 'predatory' and 'blameworthy soul'; the rational is simply the rational power.
2
Identify each power's function
For each power, note what it governs: concupiscent (attraction of benefits, repulsion of harms), irascible (advancing toward terrors, desire for domination), rational (perceiving truths, distinguishing good from bad).
3
Reflect on the summary sentence
The passage opens with a summary of the four virtues and their extremes. Notice how the extremes are paired with each virtue before the powers are explained.
This preview sets the stage for the detailed discussion that follows.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالعفة الحميد والفجور، وللشجاعة التهور والجبن، وللحكمة الجريزة والغباوة. للإنسان قوة شهوية هي مبدأ جذب المنافع ودفع المضار من المآكل والمشارب وغيرها وتسمى القوة البهيمية والنفس الأمارة، وقوة غضبية هي مبدأ الإقدام على الأهوال والشوق إلى التسلط والترفع وتسمى القوة السبعية والنفوس اللوامة، وقوة نطقية هي مبدأ إدراك الحقائق والشوق إلى النظر في العواقب والتمييز بين المصالح والمفاسد. وتحدث من اعتدال حركة الأولى وهي أن تكون تصرفات البهيمية على وفق اقتضاء النطقية لتسلم عن أن يستعبدها الهوى وتستخدمها اللذات. ولها طرف إفراط هو الخلاعة والفجور أي الوقوع في ازدياد اللذات على ما ينبغي، وطرف تفريط هو الخمود أي السكون عن طلب ما رخص فيه العقل والشرع من اللذات إيثارا لا خلقة. ومن اعتدال حركة السبعية الشجاعة وهي انقيادها للنطقية ليكون إقدامها على حسب الروية من غير اضطراب في الأمور الهائلة. ولها طرف إفراط هو التهور أي الإقدام على ما لا ينبغي، وتفريط هو الجبن أي الحذر عما لا ينبغي. ومن اعتدال حركة النطقية الحكمة وهي معرفة الحقائق على ما هي عليه بقدر الاستطاعة، وطرف إفراطها الجريزة وهي استعمال الفكر فيما لا ينبغي ولا على ما ينبغي، وطرف تفريطها الغباوة وهي تعطيل الفكر بالإرادة والوقوف عن اكتساب العلوم. فالأوساط فضائل والأطراف رذائل. وإذا امتزجت الفضائل حصل من اجتماعها حالة متشابهة هي العدالة. فأصول الفضائل العفة والشجاعة والحكمة والعدالة. ولكل منها شعب وفروع مذكورة في كتب الأخلاق وكذا الرذائل الست.
For chastity: praiseworthy and debauchery; for courage: recklessness and cowardice; for wisdom: cunning and stupidity. The human has a concupiscent power which is the principle of attracting benefits and repelling harms from foods, drinks, and others, and it is called the bestial power and the commanding soul; and an irascible power which is the principle of advancing toward terrors and longing for domination and elevation, and it is called the predatory power and the blameworthy soul; and a rational power which is the principle of perceiving truths and longing for consideration of consequences and distinguishing between benefits and harms. From the moderation of the movement of the first [power] arises [temperance], which is that the actions of the bestial [power] be in accordance with the requirement of the rational [power] so that it is safe from being enslaved by passion and used by pleasures. It has an extreme of excess which is licentiousness and debauchery, i.e., falling into increasing pleasures in a way that is not proper; and an extreme of deficiency which is insensibility, i.e., abstention from seeking pleasures permitted by intellect and law out of preference, not nature. From the moderation of the movement of the predatory [power] arises courage, which is its submission to the rational [power] so that its advance be according to deliberation without agitation in terrible matters. It has an extreme of excess which is recklessness, i.e., advancing toward what is not proper; and deficiency which is cowardice, i.e., caution against what is not proper. From the moderation of the movement of the rational [power] arises wisdom, which is knowledge of truths as they are according to capacity. Its extreme of excess is cunning, which is using thought in what is not proper and not in the proper manner; its extreme of deficiency is stupidity, which is disabling thought deliberately and stopping from acquiring sciences. So the middle states are virtues and the extremes are vices. When the virtues blend, a resembling state results from their combination, which is justice. So the roots of virtues are chastity, courage, wisdom, and justice. Each has branches and subdivisions mentioned in books of ethics, and likewise the six vices.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage defines the four cardinal virtues (chastity, courage, wisdom, justice) and their corresponding vices of excess and deficiency. It begins by stating the tripartite division of the soul's powers: the concupiscent (shahwiyya), irascible (ghadabiyya), and rational (natiqiyya). For each power, virtue is the mean between two extremes. The concupiscent power's virtue is temperance ('iffa), with excess being licentiousness and deficiency being insensibility. The irascible power's virtue is courage (shaja'a), with excess being recklessness and deficiency being cowardice. The rational power's virtue is wisdom (hikma), with excess being cunning and deficiency being stupidity. The passage then explains that these middle states are virtues and the extremes are vices. When the three virtues are combined, they produce a fourth virtue: justice ('adala). Finally, it notes that each virtue has many branches detailed in ethics books, and likewise there are six vices (the two extremes for each virtue).

Key Terms
القوة الشهوية
القوة الغضبية
القوة النطقية
القوة البهيمية
النفس الأمارة
القوة السبعية
النفوس اللوامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That there are only two powers (body and soul) or that virtues are defined without reference to the soul's faculties.
Correction: Sanusi explicitly identifies three distinct powers, each with a specific function, and virtues arise from their moderation.

Checkpoint
Which power is associated with the 'commanding soul' (al-nafs al-ammara)?
A) Concupiscent power
B) Irascible power
C) Rational power
D) All three

Key Takeaway
The three powers of the soul—concupiscent, irascible, and rational—are the raw materials from which virtues are shaped through moderation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of these three powers — concupiscent, irascible, or rational — do you find most dominant in your daily decisions? How does that imbalance show itself? What one practical step can you take today to restore the rational power's guidance?
?
The passage describes the rational power as the principle of distinguishing benefits from harms. Think of a recent choice you made that was driven by desire or anger rather than reason. How could you have used your rational power differently? What would you do differently next time?
?
The concupiscent power is called the commanding soul. In what area of your life does it command you rather than you commanding it? What would it look like to bring it under the rule of moderation? Write down one concrete change you will make this week to temper that desire.

Temperance and Its Extremes
Sanusi defines temperance and its two extremes of excess and deficiency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how temperance (chastity) is defined as the moderation of the concupiscent power, and what its excess (debauchery) and deficiency (insensibility) entail.

Core Idea
Temperance means the concupiscent power obeys reason.

After outlining the three soul powers, Sanusi now defines the first virtue: temperance. He explains how it arises from the moderation of the concupiscent power and identifies its two opposite vices. This lesson clarifies the nuanced meaning of the mean in Islamic ethics.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of temperance
Find where Sanusi states that temperance arises from the moderation of the concupiscent power. Note the condition: the concupiscent must act according to the rational power.
The phrase 'على وفق اقتضاء النطقية' is key—temperance requires reason's guidance.
2
Identify the two extremes
Read the description of licentiousness (excess) and insensibility (deficiency). Notice the nuance: insensibility is not simply abstaining, but doing so out of preference, not innate lack of desire.
3
Reflect on the middle state
Consider why the middle is virtuous: it is neither indulging every desire nor rejecting permissible pleasures. The rational power sets the boundary.
This mirrors Aristotle's doctrine of the mean but is framed within Islamic revelation.

Courage and Its Extremes
Sanusi defines courage and its extremes of recklessness and cowardice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how courage is defined as the moderation of the irascible power, and its extremes of recklessness and cowardice.

Core Idea
Courage means the irascible power follows reason in the face of danger.

Sanusi now turns to the second virtue: courage. He explains that true courage arises when the irascible power is moderated by reason, and he defines its two perversions. This lesson explores the psychological basis for bravery in the Islamic ethical tradition.

Guided Path
1
Find the definition of courage
Locate the sentence where courage is said to arise from the moderation of the irascible power. Note the phrase 'انقيادها للنطقية'—submission to reason.
2
Identify the extremes
Read the descriptions of recklessness and cowardice. Note that recklessness is advancing when it is not appropriate, cowardice is being cautious when it is not appropriate.
3
Connect to daily life
Think of situations where courage would be the mean: e.g., defending against oppression without being foolhardy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالعفة الحميد والفجور، وللشجاعة التهور والجبن، وللحكمة الجريزة والغباوة. للإنسان قوة شهوية هي مبدأ جذب المنافع ودفع المضار من المآكل والمشارب وغيرها وتسمى القوة البهيمية والنفس الأمارة، وقوة غضبية هي مبدأ الإقدام على الأهوال والشوق إلى التسلط والترفع وتسمى القوة السبعية والنفوس اللوامة، وقوة نطقية هي مبدأ إدراك الحقائق والشوق إلى النظر في العواقب والتمييز بين المصالح والمفاسد. وتحدث من اعتدال حركة الأولى وهي أن تكون تصرفات البهيمية على وفق اقتضاء النطقية لتسلم عن أن يستعبدها الهوى وتستخدمها اللذات. ولها طرف إفراط هو الخلاعة والفجور أي الوقوع في ازدياد اللذات على ما ينبغي، وطرف تفريط هو الخمود أي السكون عن طلب ما رخص فيه العقل والشرع من اللذات إيثارا لا خلقة. ومن اعتدال حركة السبعية الشجاعة وهي انقيادها للنطقية ليكون إقدامها على حسب الروية من غير اضطراب في الأمور الهائلة. ولها طرف إفراط هو التهور أي الإقدام على ما لا ينبغي، وتفريط هو الجبن أي الحذر عما لا ينبغي. ومن اعتدال حركة النطقية الحكمة وهي معرفة الحقائق على ما هي عليه بقدر الاستطاعة، وطرف إفراطها الجريزة وهي استعمال الفكر فيما لا ينبغي ولا على ما ينبغي، وطرف تفريطها الغباوة وهي تعطيل الفكر بالإرادة والوقوف عن اكتساب العلوم. فالأوساط فضائل والأطراف رذائل. وإذا امتزجت الفضائل حصل من اجتماعها حالة متشابهة هي العدالة. فأصول الفضائل العفة والشجاعة والحكمة والعدالة. ولكل منها شعب وفروع مذكورة في كتب الأخلاق وكذا الرذائل الست.
For chastity: praiseworthy and debauchery; for courage: recklessness and cowardice; for wisdom: cunning and stupidity. The human has a concupiscent power which is the principle of attracting benefits and repelling harms from foods, drinks, and others, and it is called the bestial power and the commanding soul; and an irascible power which is the principle of advancing toward terrors and longing for domination and elevation, and it is called the predatory power and the blameworthy soul; and a rational power which is the principle of perceiving truths and longing for consideration of consequences and distinguishing between benefits and harms. From the moderation of the movement of the first [power] arises [temperance], which is that the actions of the bestial [power] be in accordance with the requirement of the rational [power] so that it is safe from being enslaved by passion and used by pleasures. It has an extreme of excess which is licentiousness and debauchery, i.e., falling into increasing pleasures in a way that is not proper; and an extreme of deficiency which is insensibility, i.e., abstention from seeking pleasures permitted by intellect and law out of preference, not nature. From the moderation of the movement of the predatory [power] arises courage, which is its submission to the rational [power] so that its advance be according to deliberation without agitation in terrible matters. It has an extreme of excess which is recklessness, i.e., advancing toward what is not proper; and deficiency which is cowardice, i.e., caution against what is not proper. From the moderation of the movement of the rational [power] arises wisdom, which is knowledge of truths as they are according to capacity. Its extreme of excess is cunning, which is using thought in what is not proper and not in the proper manner; its extreme of deficiency is stupidity, which is disabling thought deliberately and stopping from acquiring sciences. So the middle states are virtues and the extremes are vices. When the virtues blend, a resembling state results from their combination, which is justice. So the roots of virtues are chastity, courage, wisdom, and justice. Each has branches and subdivisions mentioned in books of ethics, and likewise the six vices.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage defines the four cardinal virtues (chastity, courage, wisdom, justice) and their corresponding vices of excess and deficiency. It begins by stating the tripartite division of the soul's powers: the concupiscent (shahwiyya), irascible (ghadabiyya), and rational (natiqiyya). For each power, virtue is the mean between two extremes. The concupiscent power's virtue is temperance ('iffa), with excess being licentiousness and deficiency being insensibility. The irascible power's virtue is courage (shaja'a), with excess being recklessness and deficiency being cowardice. The rational power's virtue is wisdom (hikma), with excess being cunning and deficiency being stupidity. The passage then explains that these middle states are virtues and the extremes are vices. When the three virtues are combined, they produce a fourth virtue: justice ('adala). Finally, it notes that each virtue has many branches detailed in ethics books, and likewise there are six vices (the two extremes for each virtue).

Key Terms
الشجاعة
التهور
الجبن
القوة الغضبية
النطقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the extreme of deficiency for courage?
A) Recklessness
B) Insensibility
C) Cowardice
D) Licentiousness

Key Takeaway
Courage is not the absence of fear, but the rational control of the irascible power to act appropriately in danger.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation that terrifies you, does your immediate impulse lean more toward reckless action or fearful withdrawal? How does this lesson challenge you to bring your response under the authority of your rational soul?
?
Think of a fear you've been avoiding that you know you should confront. What specific steps can you take to ensure your advance is 'according to deliberation' and not based on agitation?
?
In what area of your life have you mistaken impulsiveness for courage? What would it look like for your irascible power to truly submit to reason in that area starting today?

Wisdom and Its Extremes
Sanusi defines wisdom and its extremes of cunning and stupidity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how wisdom is defined as the moderation of the rational power, and its extremes of cunning and stupidity.

Core Idea
Wisdom is the rational power knowing truths as they are.

The third virtue is wisdom, which moderates the rational power itself. Sanusi defines it as knowledge of truths according to capacity, then identifies its distortions: cunning (excess of thought) and stupidity (deficiency of thought). This lesson distinguishes true wisdom from mere cleverness or ignorance.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of wisdom
Find where wisdom is defined as the moderation of the rational power. Note the phrase 'معرفة الحقائق على ما هي عليه بقدر الاستطاعة'.
Wisdom is not omniscience but knowledge according to human capacity.
2
Understand the extremes
Cunning is using thought improperly and for wrong ends; stupidity is the deliberate neglect of thought and stopping the acquisition of sciences.
Stupidity here is not innate but a willful disabling of the intellect.
3
Reflect on the mean
Consider the balance: wisdom is the proper use of intellect to know truth, avoiding both overthinking and intellectual laziness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

What is the extreme of excess for wisdom according to Sanusi?
A) Stupidity

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you used your intellect to manipulate a situation for your own gain, rather than to seek the truth? What would it cost you to repent from that cunning and pursue wisdom instead?
?
Think of a difficult truth you have been avoiding. What step will you take today to stop disabling your thought (stupidity) and begin acquiring that knowledge?
?
Which extreme of the rational power do you naturally lean toward—cunning or stupidity? What is one concrete change you will make this week to move toward the middle path of wisdom?

The Four Cardinal Virtues and Justice
Sanusi states the mean principle and introduces justice as the blend of the three virtues.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the middle states are virtues and the extremes are vices, and that justice arises from the blend of the three primary virtues.

Core Idea
Justice is the harmony of all virtues.

Sanusi concludes by summarizing the virtue ethics framework: the mean is virtue, extremes are vice. He then reveals that when the three primary virtues blend, a fourth virtue—justice—emerges. This lesson ties together the entire ethical system presented in the passage.

Guided Path
1
Read the concluding summary
Find the sentence 'فالأوساط فضائل والأطراف رذائل'. This is the core principle of the mean.
2
Understand the origin of justice
Read how justice arises from the blending of the three virtues. Note the phrase 'حالة متشابهة' — a resembling state.
Justice is not a separate power but the harmonious combination of the other three.
3
Note the branches
The passage ends by stating that each virtue and its vices have many branches detailed in ethics books.
This points to a larger tradition of virtue ethics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That justice is a moderation of a specific power like the others.
Correction: Justice is not a moderation of a separate power but the harmonious combination of all three virtues.

Checkpoint
How does justice arise according to the passage?
A) From moderation of the rational power
B) From blending the three virtues
C) From avoiding extremes
D) From the irascible power

Key Takeaway
The four cardinal virtues—chastity, courage, wisdom, and justice—form the roots of all ethical excellence, with the first three being means and justice their harmonious integration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Of the three virtues—chastity, courage, and wisdom—which one are you most lacking? How does that imbalance prevent you from acting justly in your daily life? What is one concrete step you can take today to move toward the mean in that area?
?
Think of a recent situation where you acted from an extreme rather than the middle. Which vice did you fall into? How can you return to the mean, and what will you do differently next time to blend the virtues?
?
Justice is described as the blend of virtues. In your closest relationships, are you blending patience, honesty, and compassion, or are you relying on one virtue at the expense of others? What specific action can you take today to harmonize them and become more just?

Three Types of Dreams
Al-Taftazani enumerates the three types of dreams.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three categories of dreams according to Islamic dream interpretation: true dreams, dreams requiring interpretation, and confused nightmares.

Core Idea
Dreams are classified by how the imaginal faculty handles the original image.

In this lesson, we examine the opening of al-Taftazani's discussion on dreams. He presents a concise classification: true dreams where the image remains unchanged, dreams requiring interpretation when the image is altered but traceable, and confused nightmares when no meaningful correspondence exists.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get an overview of the three categories. Then read the translation to confirm your understanding.
The passage is a single sentence that lists all three types.
2
Identify each type
Identify the criteria for each dream type: condition of the image and role of the imaginal faculty.
Notice the terms: فرؤيا صادقة, فرؤيا تعبير, أضغاث أحلام.
3
Reflect on the order
Consider why true dreams come first, then those needing interpretation, then confused nightmares. What does this hierarchy imply about their reliability?

Source Text

قال (وقالوا في إدراكاتها المتعلقة بالنوم : إنها تتصل بعالم الغيب لركود الحواس، فيحصل لها صورة إدراكية جزئية في نفسها، أو بجعل المتخيلة، فإن بقيت على حالها، بحيث لا تتفاوت في المجعولة إلا بالكلية والجزئية، وتأدت إلى الحس المشترك فرؤيا صادقة، وإن تصرف فيها المتخيلة بتبديل الصور، فإن أمكن أن تعاد إلى الأصل بضرب من التحليل، فرؤيا تعبير، وإلا فأضغاث أحلام). إشارة إلى القسم الثاني، وبيان ذلك أن النفس لاشتغالها بالتفكر فيما تورد عليها الحواس، قلما تفرغ للاتصال بالجواهر الروحانية، فعند ركود الحواس بسبب انحباس الروح الحاملة لقوة الحس عنها، تتصل النفس بتلك الجواهر وينطبع فيها ما فيها من صور الأشياء، سيما ما هو أليق بتلك النفس من أحوالها، وأحوال ما يقرب منها من الأهل والولد والمال والبلد، وتلك الصور قد تكون جزئية في نفسها وقد تكون كلية تحاكيها المتخيلة بصور جزئية، ثم تنطبع في الخيال وتنتقل إلى الحس المشترك فتصير مشاهدة فإن كانت الصورة المشاهدة باقية على حالها، بحيث لا تفاوت فيما جعلته المتخيلة جزئية إلا بالكلية والجزئية، كانت الرؤيا غنية عن التعبير وإلا فإن كان هناك مناسبة يمكن الوقوف عليها، كما إذا صور المعنى بصورة لازمة أو ضده مثلاً، فهي رؤيا تعبر، ومعنى التعبير هو التحليل بالعكس لفعل التخيل، حتى ينتهي إلى مشاهدته النفس عند الاتصال بعالم الغيب فإن المتخيلة لما فيها من غريزة المحاكاة، والانتقال تترك ما أخذت، وتورد شبهه أو ضده أو مناسبه، وربما تبدل ذلك إلى آخر وآخر، وهكذا إلى حين اليقظة، فالمعبر ينظر في الحاضر أنه صورة لأية صورة وتلك لأية صورة أخرى، إلى أن ينتهي إلى الصورة التي أدركتها النفس، وإن لم يكن هناك مناسبة توقف عليه، فتلك الرؤيا تعد في أضغاث الأحلام، وقد يقع ذلك بأسباب أخر، مثل أن تبقى صورة المحسوس في الخيال، فتنتقل في النوم إلى الحس المشترك، ومثل أن تألف الفكرة صورة فتنتقل منها عند النوم إلى الخيال، ثم منه إلى الحس المشترك، ومثل أن يتغير مزاج الروح الحامل للقوة المتخيلة، فتتغير أفعالها بحسب تلك التغيرات، فمن غلب على مزاجه الصفراء حاكته بالأشياء الصفراء، والدم فبالحمراء، والسوداء فبالسوداء، والبلغم فبالجمد والثلج.
He said (and they said regarding perceptions related to sleep: They [the souls] connect to the world of the unseen due to the stagnation of the senses, so a partial perceptual image occurs in themselves, or by the activity of the imaginal faculty. If it remains in its state such that it does not differ in the constructed image except in terms of universality and particularity, and it reaches the common sense, it is a true dream. If the imaginal faculty manipulates it by changing images, then if it is possible to return it to the original by some analysis, it is a dream requiring interpretation; otherwise it is confused nightmares.) A reference to the second division, and its explanation is that the soul, due to its preoccupation with contemplating what the senses bring to it, rarely finds time to connect with the spiritual substances. But when the senses stagnate due to the confinement of the spirit that carries the sensory power away from them, the soul connects to those substances and imprints within itself what they contain of images of things, especially what is most appropriate to that soul regarding its states and the states of those close to it such as family, children, wealth, and country. Those images may be particular in themselves or universal, which the imaginal faculty replicates with particular images, then they are imprinted in the imagination and transferred to the common sense, becoming visible. If the visible image remains in its state such that what the imaginal faculty made particular differs only in terms of universality and particularity, the dream is free of need for interpretation. Otherwise, if there is an appropriate correspondence that can be identified — as when they picture a meaning with a necessary image or its opposite, for example — it is a dream to be interpreted. The meaning of interpretation is reverse analysis of the act of imagination until it reaches the soul's witnessing upon connection to the world of the unseen. For the imaginal faculty, having in it an instinct for imitation and transition, leaves what it took and presents its likeness, opposite, or corresponding thing, and perhaps changes it to another and another, and so on until waking. The interpreter looks at the present image to see which image it represents, and that one to which other, until reaching the image that the soul perceived. If there is no identifiable correspondence, that dream is counted among confused nightmares. That may occur for other reasons, such as the image of a perceived object remaining in the imagination and being transferred in sleep to the common sense, or such as thought becoming familiar with an image and transferring from it during sleep to the imagination and then from it to the common sense, or such as the temperament of the spirit carrying the imaginal power changing, so its actions change according to those changes: whoever's temperament is dominated by choler (yellow bile), the imaginal faculty imitates with yellow things; blood with red; black bile (melancholy) with black; phlegm with ice and snow.
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage is a definition-and-explanation passage. It begins with a quotation from the original text classifying dreams into three types: true dreams (ru'ya sadiqa), dreams requiring interpretation (ru'ya ta'bir), and confused nightmares (adghath ahlam). The commentator then explains the mechanism behind these dreams. First, during sleep the senses are at rest, allowing the soul to connect to spiritual substances (jawahir ruhaniya) and receive images from the unseen world. These images may be particular or universal. The imaginal faculty (mutakhayyila) may then replicate universal images as particular ones, and these are transferred to the common sense (hiss mushtarak), making them 'seen'. If the image remains unchanged, the dream is true and needs no interpretation. If the imaginal faculty alters the image but the original can be reconstructed through logical analysis (e.g., opposites or necessary concomitants), the dream requires interpretation. If no such correspondence exists, it is a confused nightmare. The commentator then lists other causes of confused dreams: leftover sensory images, thoughts during sleep, and changes in bodily temperament affecting the imaginal faculty (e.g., excess bile causing perception of corresponding colors). The passage is structured as exposition with examples, preserving the author's emphasis on the real basis of dreams in spiritual connection.

Key Terms
رؤيا صادقة
رؤيا تعبير
أضغاث أحلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All dreams are either entirely symbolic or entirely meaningless.
Correction: The passage shows a spectrum: true dreams are literal, interpretation dreams are symbolic but decipherable, and confused nightmares are meaningless.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what characterizes a dream that requires interpretation (تعبير)?
A) The imaginal faculty does not alter the image at all.
B) The image is altered but can be traced back to its original meaning through analysis.
C) The image is completely nonsensical.
D) The dream is caused by an imbalance of bodily humors.

Key Takeaway
Dreams are classified into three types based on whether the imaginal faculty preserves, alters but reversibly, or distorts the spiritual image.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage teaches that true dreams occur when the image remains unchanged. When was the last time you had a dream that felt strikingly clear and literal? Did you treat it as a potential message from the unseen, or did you brush it aside? What would it take for you to start recording and reflecting on such dreams?
?
Dreams requiring interpretation involve symbols that can be traced back to an original meaning. Are there dreams from your past that you now realize were symbolic but you never tried to interpret? How might this lesson change your responsibility toward those dreams?
?
Confused nightmares are those whose images cannot be traced back. Do you find yourself sometimes trying to extract meaning from chaotic dreams, forcing an interpretation where there is none? How can this lesson help you be honest with yourself about when a dream is truly meaningful versus when it is just mental noise?

Mechanism of Dream Formation
Al-Taftazani explains the mechanism of dream formation and the role of the imaginal faculty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the soul connects to spiritual substances during sleep and how the imaginal faculty transforms images to produce different types of dreams.

Core Idea
Dreams originate from the soul's connection to the unseen, then are shaped by the imaginal faculty.

Al-Taftazani provides a detailed explanation of how dreams are formed, starting with the soul's connection to spiritual substances when the senses are dormant. He then describes how the imaginal faculty either preserves or alters the images, leading to the three dream types.

Guided Path
1
Understand the soul's connection
Notice that during wakefulness, the soul is busy with sensory input, but during sleep the senses stagnate, allowing the soul to connect to spiritual substances (jawahir ruhaniya).
2
Follow the image path
Images from spiritual substances are imprinted in the soul. They may be universal or particular. The imaginal faculty replicates universal images as particular ones. Then these images go to imagination, then to common sense, becoming visible.
Key terms: متخيلة, خيال, حس مشترك.
3
Differentiate outcomes
If the image remains unchanged, it's a true dream. If changed but traceable, it's an interpretation dream. If changed beyond recognition, it's a confused nightmare. The imaginal faculty's instinct for imitation and transition causes the changes.
The commentator gives examples: opposites, necessary concomitants.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Checkpoint
What is the role of the imaginal faculty (المتخيلة) in dream formation according to the passage?
A) It connects the soul to spiritual substances.
B) It replicates universal images as particular images and may alter them.
C) It sends images directly to the common sense without change.
D) It interprets dreams after waking.

Key Takeaway
The soul's connection to spiritual substances and the imaginal faculty's handling of images determine whether a dream is true, interpretable, or confused.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your soul connects to spiritual substances during sleep, yet you rarely pause to consider whether your dreams might carry real spiritual insight. What concrete step will you take tonight to become more attentive to the possibility that a dream is from the unseen?
?
You often dismiss a confusing dream as meaningless or just a 'bad dream'. But Al-Taftazani warns that the imaginal faculty constantly alters images until waking. When you next have an unsettling dream, what effort will you make to trace it back—through its 'opposite' or 'necessary images'—to what your soul might have originally perceived?
?
The passage states that even the prophetically guided dream can become obscured by the imaginal faculty's instinct of imitation. Are you willing to humble yourself and seek help from someone truthful to interpret a dream, or do you trust your own first impression? What does your answer reveal about your reliance on your own intellect compared to seeking knowledge from others?

Additional Causes of Confused Dreams
Al-Taftazani enumerates additional causes of confused nightmares.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will recognize the physical and psychological factors that can produce confused nightmares, including residual sensory images, persistent thoughts, and imbalances in bodily humors.

Core Idea
Not all confused dreams stem from spiritual causes; physical and psychological factors also play a role.

After explaining the primary mechanism of dreams, al-Taftazani lists additional causes for confused nightmares. These include leftover sensory images, thoughts carried into sleep, and changes in bodily temperament affecting the imaginal faculty. This shows a holistic view combining spiritual psychology with humoral medicine.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three additional causes
Read the passage and note the three specific causes: residual sensory images, persistent thoughts, and temperament changes.
2
Understand each mechanism
For each cause, see how it leads to images reaching the common sense during sleep without a spiritual origin.
The passage gives examples for temperament: choler (yellow) leads to yellow images, etc.
3
Connect to humoral theory
Reflect on how the four humors (blood, phlegm, yellow bile, black bile) affect the imaginal faculty's output, resulting in dream content influenced by physical state.
This shows the integration of medical theory with dream interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage is a definition-and-explanation passage. It begins with a quotation from the original text classifying dreams into three types: true dreams (ru'ya sadiqa), dreams requiring interpretation (ru'ya ta'bir), and confused nightmares (adghath ahlam). The commentator then explains the mechanism behind these dreams. First, during sleep the senses are at rest, allowing the soul to connect to spiritual substances (jawahir ruhaniya) and receive images from the unseen world. These images may be particular or universal. The imaginal faculty (mutakhayyila) may then replicate universal images as particular ones, and these are transferred to the common sense (hiss mushtarak), making them 'seen'. If the image remains unchanged, the dream is true and needs no interpretation. If the imaginal faculty alters the image but the original can be reconstructed through logical analysis (e.g., opposites or necessary concomitants), the dream requires interpretation. If no such correspondence exists, it is a confused nightmare. The commentator then lists other causes of confused dreams: leftover sensory images, thoughts during sleep, and changes in bodily temperament affecting the imaginal faculty (e.g., excess bile causing perception of corresponding colors). The passage is structured as exposition with examples, preserving the author's emphasis on the real basis of dreams in spiritual connection.

Key Terms
صفراء
دم
سوداء
بلغم
مزاج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Confused nightmares always have profound hidden meanings.
Correction: The passage shows that they can be caused by simple physical or psychological factors like leftover sensations or humoral imbalances, and thus may be meaningless.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens when the temperament of the spirit carrying the imaginal power changes?
A) The soul connects more strongly to spiritual substances.
B) The imaginal faculty's actions change, often producing images matching the dominant humor.
C) The dream becomes automatically true.
D) The interpreter can always find a correspondence.

Key Takeaway
Confused nightmares can arise from sensory leftovers, thoughts, or bodily imbalances, not just from the soul's connection to the unseen.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a frightening dream, do you immediately assume it is a warning from the unseen, or do you honestly consider whether it might stem from your physical state or a lingering thought? How would your response change if you took the latter possibility seriously?
?
The text describes how temperament imbalances produce specific dream imagery. Think about your own dreams: can you trace any recurring images to your diet, stress, or health? What would it mean for you to take responsibility for your dreams by attending to your body?
?
If most confused dreams have mundane causes, what does that say about your tendency to seek deep spiritual meanings in daily experiences? Are you perhaps neglecting the physical and psychological factors that God has placed in creation as natural explanations?

Wakeful Perception of the Unseen
Al-Taftazani describes wakeful perception via sensory proxies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that according to al-Taftazani, the soul can perceive hidden things while awake through detachment from the sensory world and engagement of the imaginal faculty, and that such perception occurs via specific sensory proxies.

Core Idea
The unseen can be perceived in wakefulness through detachment from the senses.

In this passage, al-Taftazani reports the view that the soul can access the unseen even while awake, not only in dreams or trance. He explains that by detaching from the sensory world and using the imaginal faculty to extract the common sense, one can become aware of hidden things through various means such as hearing sounds, seeing written words, or receiving discourse from unseen beings. This challenges the common assumption that the unseen is only perceived during sleep.

Guided Path
1

Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Notice the key terms and the structure of the description.
The passage ends abruptly; consider that it may continue on the next page.

The common sense (al-hiss al-mushtarak) is a faculty that combines data from external senses.
3
List the modes of receiving unseen knowledge
The passage lists hearing a pleasant or tremendous sound, seeing something written, or discourse from a human, angel, jinn, or a caller from the unseen. Note that these are all sensory proxies.
4

Reflect on the common misunderstanding that this only happens in sleep. The passage explicitly says 'regarding wakeful' and describes the soul detaching while awake.
This reinforces that the unseen is accessible in full consciousness.

Dream Interpretation and the Condition of Suitability
Al-Taftazani defines dream interpretation and states when it is needed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of dream interpretation (ta'bir) and the condition that makes a dream require interpretation.

Core Idea
Dream interpretation is reverse engineering the imaginative process.

Al-Taftazani begins by laying down a foundational concept in the science of dream interpretation. He explains that not all dreams need interpretation; only those where the imagination has transformed the original perception into something else through a process of imitation and transition. This lesson unpacks his definition and the crucial role of suitability (munasaba).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to grasp the flow.
Notice the conditional structure: 'otherwise, if there is suitability...'
2
Identify the condition for interpretation
Find the condition that makes a vision require interpretation (munasaba). Understand what 'suitability' means in this context.
3
Understand the definition of ta'bir
The author defines tafsir as reverse analysis of the act of imagination. Trace the process from the waking image back to the original perception.
Key phrase: 'reverse analysis of the act of imagination.'
4
Reflect on the imaginative faculty's role
Notice how the imaginative faculty (mutakhayyila) operates by imitation and transition. This explains why dreams need interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باقية على حالها، بحيث لا تفاوت فيما جعلته المتخيلة جزئية إلا بالكلية والجزئية، كانت الرؤيا غنية عن التعبير وإلا فإن كان هناك مناسبة يمكن الوقوف عليها، كما إذا صور المعنى بصورة لازمة أو ضده مثلاً، فهي رؤيا تعبر، ومعنى التعبير هو التحليل بالعكس لفعل التخيل، حتى ينتهي إلى مشاهدته النفس عند الاتصال بعالم الغيب فإن المتخيلة لما فيها من غريزة المحاكاة، والانتقال تترك ما أخذت، وتورد شبهه أو ضده أو مناسبه، وربما تبدل ذلك إلى آخر وآخر، وهكذا إلى حين اليقظة، فالمعبر ينظر في الحاضر أنه صورة لأية صورة وتلك لأية صورة أخرى، إلى أن ينتهي إلى الصورة التي أدركتها النفس
Remaining in their state, such that there is no variation in what the imaginative faculty made particular except in universality and particularity, the vision is independent of interpretation. Otherwise, if there is a suitability that can be identified, such as when it pictures the meaning in a necessary form or its opposite, for example, then it is a vision that requires interpretation. The meaning of interpretation is the reverse analysis of the act of imagination, until it ends at what the soul witnessed when connected to the world of the unseen. For the imaginative faculty, due to its instinct of imitation and transition, leaves what it took and presents its similitude or opposite or suitable, and perhaps it changes that to another and another, and so on until waking. So the interpreter looks at the present form, that it is an image of which image, and that image of which other image, until he reaches the image that the soul perceived.
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of dream interpretation (ta'bir) and clarifies the condition under which a dream requires interpretation. The author begins by stating that if the images formed by the imaginative faculty remain without variation in their particularity except in terms of universality and particularity, then the dream is independent of interpretation—i.e., it is clear and needs no explanation. Otherwise, if there is a suitability (munasaba) that can be identified between the image and the meaning, such as when the meaning is pictured in a necessary form or its opposite, then the dream is interpretable. The author then defines ta'bir as the reverse analysis of the imaginative process, tracing back step by step until one reaches the original image that the soul perceived when connected to the unseen world. He explains that the imaginative faculty, by its instinct, imitates and transitions: it leaves what it originally took and presents a similitude, opposite, or suitable image, and may change it repeatedly until waking. The interpreter therefore examines the present form, determines what it is an image of, and continues until he reaches the image the soul actually perceived. Thus, interpretation is a backward movement from the imaginative representation to the original perception.

Key Terms
تعبير (ta'bir)
متخيلة (mutakhayyila)
مناسبة (munasaba)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All dreams require interpretation.
Correction: The author states that if the images remain without variation (except in universality/particularity), the dream is independent of interpretation. Only when there is a suitability (munasaba) does it require interpretation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what condition makes a dream need interpretation?
A) The images are completely clear and unambiguous.
B) There is a suitability (munasaba) that can be identified between the image and the meaning.
C) The soul is strong and connected to the unseen.
D) The dream comes directly from God.

Key Takeaway
Dream interpretation is the reverse analysis of the imaginative faculty's transformations, needed only when a suitable link exists between the image and the original perception.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reflect on a dream you had that seemed significant. Based on this lesson, can you identify a clear 'suitability' between the images and a possible meaning? If no, does that challenge your desire to interpret it? If yes, are you willing to seek help in tracing the images back to your soul's original perception, knowing the process is complex and requires humility?
?
The imaginative faculty distorts the original perception through imitation and transition. In your own life, where do you let your imagination deform the truth—especially about yourself or others? What would it mean for you to reverse-engineer those distortions to find the reality, as the interpreter does in dreams?
?
The interpreter works backwards step by step. When you face confusion about your purpose or spiritual state, do you prefer quick answers or are you patient enough to trace your thoughts back to their source? This lesson implies that deep understanding takes effort. Will you commit to that effort today?

Three Causes of Confused Dreams
Al-Taftazani enumerates three causes of confused dreams.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three causes of confused dreams (adghath ahlam) as explained by al-Taftazani, and how they relate to imagination, common sense, and temperament.

Core Idea
Confused dreams are not random—they arise from distinct psychic or physiological causes.

Dreams can be clear or confused. Al-Taftazani now explains what happens when a vision lacks identifiable suitability with reality. Such dreams fall into a category called 'adghath ahlam' (confused dreams). He enumerates three distinct causes, each rooted in the interaction between imagination, common sense, and bodily temperament. This passage lays out a rational explanation for why some dreams are jumbled.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get an overview. Notice the structure: a general statement followed by three 'mithal' (like) clauses.
The passage is a clear enumeration introduced by 'مثل أن' three times.
2
Identify the three causes
List the three causes as al-Taftazani presents them: (1) residual sense images transferring to common sense, (2) ideas associated with images transferring, and (3) changes in temperament affecting the imaginative faculty.
Pay attention to the sequence: each cause involves a movement between imagination and common sense.
3
Understand the physiological cause
Focus on the third cause where temperament (mizaj) alters the spirit. Note the examples given for each humor: choler, blood, black bile, phlegm, and the corresponding colors/elements seen in dreams.
This links dream content to bodily humors, a key concept in medieval Islamic medicine.
4
Reflect on the contrast
Consider how these causes differ from true visions (ru'ya) that have identifiable suitability. Confused dreams are not random but have explicable origins.
This helps consolidate the overall teaching of the section.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن لم يكن هناك مناسبة توقف عليه، فتلك الرؤيا تعد في أضغاث الأحلام، وقد يقع ذلك بأسباب أخر، مثل أن تبقى صورة المحسوس في الخيال، فتنتقل في النوم إلى الحس المشترك، ومثل أن تألف الفكرة صورة فتنتقل منها عند النوم إلى الخيال، ثم منه إلى الحس المشترك، ومثل أن يتغير مزاج الروح، الحامل للقوة المتخيلة، فتتغير أفعالها بحسب تلك التغيرات، فمن غلب على مزاجه الصفراء حاكته بالأشياء الصفراء، والدم فبالحمراء، والسوداء فبالسوداء، والبلغم فبالجمـد والثلج
And if there is no suitability that can be identified, then that vision is counted among confused dreams. And that may occur due to other causes, such as the image of a sensed object remains in the imagination, then it transfers in sleep to the common sense; and such as an idea becomes familiar with an image, then it transfers from it during sleep to the imagination, then from it to the common sense; and such as the temperament of the spirit, which carries the imaginative faculty, changes, so its actions change according to those changes. So whoever's temperament is dominated by choler, it portrays him with yellow things; blood with red things; black bile with black things; phlegm with ice and snow.
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage enumerates the causes of confused dreams (adghath ahlam). The author states that when there is no identifiable suitability, the vision is counted among confused dreams. He then lists three causes: (1) The image of a sensed object remains in the imagination and then transfers to the common sense during sleep. (2) An idea becomes associated with an image, then transfers during sleep to the imagination, then to the common sense. (3) The temperament (mizaj) of the spirit, which carries the imaginative faculty, changes, causing the actions of the faculty to change accordingly. For example, if choler dominates, the person sees yellow things; if blood dominates, red; if black bile, black; if phlegm, ice and snow. Thus, the author links dream content to physiological factors.

Key Terms
أضغاث الأحلام
خيال
حس مشترك
مزاج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think confused dreams are entirely random without any cause.
Correction: Al-Taftazani provides specific physical and psychic causes, showing that even confused dreams have identifiable origins.

Checkpoint

A) The image of a sensed object remains in the imagination and transfers to the common sense.
B) An idea becomes associated with an image and transfers during sleep.
C) Change in the temperament of the spirit.
D) Contact with the unseen world through a strong soul.

Key Takeaway
Confused dreams have identifiable causes rooted in the interplay of imagination, memory, and bodily temperament.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever noticed that your dreams reflect your physical state—like when you are sick or stressed? What does Al-Taftazani's third cause (change in temperament) suggest about the connection between your body and your soul? How might this lead you to take better care of your health, knowing it affects your inner life?
?

What does this reveal about how your mind processes daily experiences? How can this awareness help you discern which dreams to pay attention to?
?
If confused dreams arise from internal causes, how does that challenge the assumption that all dreams have external or supernatural meanings? How might this lesson change the way you consider dreams in your life—are you too quick to ascribe significance to them, or too dismissive? Commit to evaluating your dreams with this new understanding.

The Soul's Waking Connection to the Unseen
Introduction to waking perceptions of the unseen.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the soul can perceive the unseen while awake through disconnecting from the senses and connecting to the higher principles.

Core Idea
The awake soul can contact the unseen by disengaging the common sense from external senses.

Al-Taftazani begins by describing a remarkable phenomenon: the soul, when strong, can detach from the sensory world and gain knowledge of hidden things. This can happen through voices, writings, or communications from beings like angels, jinn, or unseen callers. This lesson explores the initial explanation and examples of such waking perceptions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on waking perception
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the phenomenon.
2
Understand the mechanism
Notice how the soul and imaginative faculty work together to withdraw the common sense.
3
Explore examples
Identify the types of perceptions: hearing, writing, speech from various beings.
4
Reflect on the scope
The author says wonders of souls are too many to count.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال وقالوا فيما يتعلق باليقظة : إن النفس قد تقوى على الانقطاع عن عالم الحس والاتصال بعالم الغيب والمتخيلة على استخلاص الحس المشترك عن الحواس الظاهرة، فتطلع على بعض المغيبات، وربما يكون ذلك بسماع صوت لذيذ أو هائل أو مكتوباً، أو تخاطباً من إنسان، أو ملك، أو جنى، أو هاتف غيب، وقد يقع بعض ذلك عند ضعف القوى الحسية لمرض أو مجاهدة، وعند دهشة الحس، أو تحير الخيال بمثل سرعة عدو، أو تأمل شفاف مرعش البصر، أو غلبة خوف، أو نحو ذلك . وبالجملة : فعجائب النفوس أظهر من أن تخفى وأكثر من أن تحصى. وأما الكلام في الأسباب. هذا هو القسم الثالث وهو غرائب تتعلق بالإدراكات حالة اليقظة. وذلك أن النفس قد تكون كاملة القوة، فتكتفي بالمتجاذبين، فلا يمنعها الاشتغال بتدبير الاتصال بالمبادىء، أي المجردات العلوية المفارقة والمتخيلة، أيضاً قد تكون قوية بحيث تقدر على استخلاص الحس المشترك عن الحواس الظاهرة، فلا يبعد أن يقع لمثل هذه النفس في اليقظة اتصال بالمبادىء، فينطبع فيها صور بعض المغيبات مما كان أو سيكون، ثم يفيض الأثر إلى المتخيلة، ثم ينتقل إلى الحس المشترك فربما يكون ذلك بسماع صوت لذيذ أو هائل، وربما يرد مكتوباً على لوح، أو تخاطباً من إنسان، أو ملك، أو جنى، أو هاتف غيب، أو نحو ذلك، وقد يكون مشاهدة صور ما لا حضور له عند الحس، لا لشرف النفس وكمال قوتها بل لفساد في الآلات التي يستعملها العقل، كما في المرض والجنون، أو لاستيلاء أمر يدهش الحس، ويحير الخيال، كالعدو سرعة، وكتأمل شيء شفاف مرعش للبصر، مدهش إياه الشفيفة، كسواد براق، أو لغلبة خوف أو ظن، أو وهم تعين التخيل، وقد يكون ذلك بالرياضات المضعفة للقوى العائقة للنفس عن اتصالها بالمباديء الجاذبة إياها إلى جانب السفليات، إلى غير من الأسباب المؤثرة عند الفلاسفة والعادية عندنا والخالق هو الله تعالى. إن الذين قالوا ربنا الله ثم استقاموا تتنزل عليهم الملائكة ألا تخافوا ولا تحزنوا وابشروا بالجنة التي كنتم توعدون، نحن أولياؤكم في الحياة الدنيا وفي الآخرة ولكم فيها ما تشتهي أنفسكم ولكم فيها ما تدعون نزلاً من غفور رحيم
He said: And they said regarding wakefulness: The soul may become strong in disconnecting from the world of sense and connecting to the world of the unseen, and the imaginative faculty in withdrawing the common sense from the external senses, so it gains knowledge of some hidden things. Perhaps that occurs by hearing a pleasant or terrifying voice, or a written form, or speech from a human, an angel, a jinn, or an unseen caller. And some of that may occur due to weakness of the sensory powers from illness or spiritual struggle, and due to astonishment of the sense, or confusion of the imagination such as from the speed of an enemy, or from contemplation of a transparent thing that dazzles the sight, or from overwhelming fear, or the like. In summary: the wonders of souls are too apparent to be hidden and too numerous to be counted. And as for speech about the causes: This is the third section, which is wonders related to perceptions in the state of wakefulness. That is, the soul may be complete in power, so that occupation with managing the body does not prevent it from connecting to the principles—i.e., the separated higher abstract entities—and the imaginative faculty. Also, it may be strong in that it can withdraw the common sense from the external senses, so it is not improbable that for such a soul in wakefulness there occurs a connection to the principles, and there are imprinted in it images of some hidden things—what was or will be—then the effect flows to the imaginative faculty, then transfers to the common sense. So perhaps that is by hearing a pleasant or terrifying voice, and perhaps it comes as writing on a tablet, or speech from a human, an angel, a jinn, or an unseen caller, or the like. And it may be the observation of images of what is not present to the sense, not due to the nobility of the soul and perfection of its power but rather due to corruption in the instruments that reason uses, as in illness and madness, or due to the domination of an affair that astonishes the sense and confuses the imagination, such as the speed of an enemy, and such as contemplating a transparent thing that dazzles the sight, astonishing it with its transparency, like a gleaming blackness, or due to overwhelming fear or conjecture or illusion that determines imagination. And that may occur through spiritual exercises that weaken the forces that hinder the soul from its connection to the principles that attract it to the side of lower things, and other such causes that are effective according to the philosophers, while for us the ordinary cause, and the Creator is Allah, the Exalted. Indeed, those who have said 'Our Lord is Allah' and then remained steadfast, the angels descend upon them, saying, 'Fear not, nor grieve, and receive good tidings of Paradise which you were promised. We are your allies in the worldly life and in the Hereafter. And therein you will have whatever your souls desire, and therein you will have whatever you call for, as hospitality from a Forgiving, Merciful One.'
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage discusses waking perceptions of the unseen. The author begins by stating that the soul can be so strong that it disconnects from the sensory world and connects to the unseen, with the imaginative faculty drawing the common sense away from the external senses, thus gaining knowledge of certain hidden things. This may occur through hearing a pleasant or terrifying voice, or through a written form, or through speech from a human, angel, jinn, or unseen caller. The author then explains that this can also happen due to weakness of the senses from illness or spiritual struggle, or due to astonishment of the senses or confusion of the imagination from fast running, or gazing at a transparent object that dazzles the sight, or overwhelming fear, etc. He summarizes that the wonders of souls are too obvious to hide and too many to count. He then adds that this is the third section—wonders related to perceptions in wakefulness. He further explains that the soul may be complete in power, so that occupation with managing the body does not prevent it from connecting to the higher abstract entities and the imaginative faculty. Or the soul may be strong in a different way, able to withdraw the common sense from the external senses, and then in wakefulness it connects to the principles, imprinting images of some hidden things—past or future—which then flow to the imaginative faculty and then to the common sense, resulting in hearing or seeing things. This can also occur not due to the soul's strength but due to corruption of the instruments used by reason, as in illness or madness, or due to an overwhelming factor that dazzles the senses and confuses the imagination. It can also occur through spiritual exercises that weaken the forces that hinder the soul from connecting to the principles that attract it to lower things. The author concludes by noting that these are causes according to philosophers, while for us (Muslims) the real cause is Allah, the Creator, and he cites a Quranic verse about angels descending on the righteous.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The soul can perceive the unseen during wakefulness by disconnecting from the senses and connecting to higher principles, resulting in various forms of hidden knowledge.

?
If the wonders of souls are 'too apparent to be hidden,' why do you often ignore or dismiss your own inner experiences? What would it take for you to trust the unseen perceptions that come to you when your senses are weak?

Causes of Waking Unseen Perceptions
Detailed enumeration of causes for waking unseen perceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the various causes of waking perceptions of the unseen, including strength of soul, corruption of bodily instruments, overwhelming factors, spiritual exercises, and the ultimate cause being Allah.

Core Idea
Waking unseen perceptions arise from either strong soul, weak instruments, or spiritual exercises, but all are ultimately from Allah.

Al-Taftazani expands on the causes of waking unseen perceptions. He presents a detailed classification: the soul may be complete in power, or there may be corruption in the instruments used by reason, or overwhelming factors may astonish the senses, or spiritual exercises may weaken hindering forces. He reminds that the real cause is Allah, citing a Quranic verse.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on causes
Begin from the section starting with 'وأما الكلام في الأسباب' to the end.
2
Understand the first cause
A soul complete in power connects directly to the principles.
3
Understand the second cause
Corruption of instruments (illness/madness).
4
Understand the third cause
Overwhelming external factors (fear, speed, dazzle).
5
Understand the fourth cause
Spiritual exercises that weaken hindering forces.
